Lottery-offices usually sufficed for the support of the manager’s family; after the Restoration some reduced women of title got two offices, worth six to ten thousand a year, employing a paid manager who often hired an assistant because the offices had to be kept open till midnight and the reports required much writing. The Comtesse promised, if her manager left, to give the place to Agathe; meantime she took Agathe as assistant with a salary of six hundred francs. Poor Agathe, obliged at the office by ten in the morning, had scarcely time for her dinner. She returned to work at seven in the evening, remaining till midnight. Joseph never, for two years, failed to fetch his mother at night and bring her back to the rue Mazarin; often he went to take her to dinner; friends frequently saw him leave the opera or some brilliant salon to be punctually at midnight at the rue Vivienne office.

Agathe soon acquired the monotonous regularity of life that supports those who have endured violent sorrows. In the morning, after doing up her room, in which there were no longer cats and little birds, she prepared breakfast at her own fire and carried it into the studio, where she ate with her son. She then arranged Joseph’s bedroom, put out the fire in her own chamber, and brought her sewing to the studio, sitting by the little iron stove and leaving if a comrade or model entered. Though she understood nothing of art, the silence suited her. She made no pretence of art appreciation; she was amazed at the importance they attached to color, composition, drawing. When the Cenacle friends or some brother-painter—Schinner, Pierre Grassou, Leon de Lora, the youthful “rapin” called Mistigris—discussed a picture, she would later examine it and find nothing to justify their fine words and disputes. She made her son’s shirts, mended his stockings, even cleaned his palette, supplied rags to wipe brushes, and kept order in the studio. Seeing how much thought his mother gave to such details, Joseph heaped attentions on her. If mother and son had no sympathy for art, they were bound by tenderness. One morning as she was petting Joseph while he sketched a large picture (finished later and never understood), she said aloud, as if casually,—

“My God! what is he doing?”

“Doing? who?”

“Philippe.”

“Oh, ah! he’s sowing his wild oats; that fellow will make something of himself by and by.”

“But he has gone through the lesson of poverty; perhaps poverty changed him. If he were prosperous he would be good—”

“You think, my dear mother, he suffered during that journey of his. You are mistaken; he kept carnival in New York just as he does here—”

“But if he is suffering at this moment, near to us, would it not be horrible?”

“Yes,” replied Joseph. “For my part, I will gladly give him some money; but I don’t want to see him; he killed our poor Descoings.”

“So,” resumed Agathe, “you would not be willing to paint his portrait?”

“For you, dear mother, I’d suffer martyrdom. I can make myself remember nothing except that he is my brother.”

“His portrait as a captain of dragoons on horseback?”

“Yes, I’ve a copy of a fine horse by Gros and I haven’t any use for it.”

“Well, then, go and see that friend of his and find out what has become of him.”

“I’ll go!”

Agathe rose; her scissors and work fell at her feet; she kissed Joseph’s head and dropped two tears on his hair.

“He is your passion, that fellow,” said the painter. “We all have our hopeless passions.”

That afternoon, about four o’clock, Joseph went to the rue du Sentier and found his brother, who had taken Giroudeau’s place. The old dragoon had been promoted to cashier of a weekly journal established by his nephew. Although Finot still owned the other newspaper, which he had divided into shares, the proprietor and editor de visu was Lousteau, the son of the sub-delegate of Issoudun on whom the Bridaus’ grandfather, Doctor Rouget, had vowed vengeance; consequently he was the nephew of Madame Hochon. To please his uncle, Finot gave Philippe the place Giroudeau was quitting, cutting off half the salary. Giroudeau audited accounts daily and carried away receipts; Coloquinte, the office boy, also watched the slippery Philippe who, however, was behaving properly. A salary of six hundred francs and five hundred from his cross sufficed him to live, living in a warm office all day and at the theatre on a free pass every evening, needing only food and a place to sleep in. Coloquinte was departing with stamped papers on his head, and Philippe was brushing his false sleeves of green linen when Joseph entered.

“Bless me, here’s the cub!” cried Philippe. “Well, we’ll go and dine together. You shall go to the opera; Florine and Florentine have got a box. I’m going with Giroudeau; you shall be of the party, and I’ll introduce you to Nathan.”

He took his leaded cane and moistened a cigar.

“I can’t accept your invitation; I am to take our mother to dine at a table d’hote.”

“Ah! how is she, the poor, dear woman?”

“She is pretty well,” answered the painter, “I have just repainted our father’s portrait, and aunt Descoings’s. I have also painted my own, and I should like to give our mother yours, in the uniform of the dragoons of the Imperial Guard.”

“Very good.”

“You will have to come and sit.”

“I’m obliged to be in this hen-coop from nine o’clock till five.”

“Two Sundays will be enough.”

“So be it, little man,” said Napoleon’s staff officer, lighting his cigar at the porter’s lamp.

When Joseph related Philippe’s position to his mother, on their way to dinner in the rue de Beaune, he felt her arm tremble in his, and joy lighted her worn face; the poor soul breathed like one relieved of a heavy weight. The next day, inspired by gratitude, she paid Joseph a number of little attentions; she decorated his studio with flowers, and bought two stands of plants. On the first Sunday when Philippe was to sit, Agathe arranged a charming breakfast in the studio. She laid it out on the table; not forgetting a flask of brandy, half full. She stayed behind a screen, making a little hole. The ex-dragoon sent his uniform the night before, and she had kissed it. When Philippe was placed, in full dress, on a straw horse, all saddled, which Joseph had hired, Agathe, fearing to betray her presence, mingled the soft sound of her tears with the brothers’ conversation. Philippe posed for two hours before and two hours after breakfast. At three o’clock, he put on ordinary clothes and, lighting a cigar, proposed to dine together in the Palais-Royal, jingling gold in his pocket as he spoke.

“No,” said Joseph, “it frightens me to see gold about you.”

“Ah! you’ll always have a bad opinion of me in this house,” cried the colonel in a thundering voice. “Can’t I save my money, too?”

“Yes, yes!” cried Agathe, coming out of her hiding-place, and kissing her son. “Let us go and dine with him, Joseph!”

Joseph dared not scold his mother. He went and dressed; Philippe took them to the Rocher de Cancale, where he gave a splendid dinner, the bill amounting to a hundred francs.

“The devil!” muttered Joseph uneasily; “with an income of eleven hundred francs you manage, like Ponchard in the ‘Dame Blance,’ to save enough to buy estates.”

“Bah, I’m on a run of luck,” answered the dragoon, who had drunk enormously.

Hearing this speech as they were on the cafe steps, and before getting into the carriage to go to the theatre—Philippe was to take his mother to the Cirque-Olympique (the only theatre her confessor allowed)—Joseph pinched his mother’s arm. She pretended to feel unwell and refused to go; Philippe took them back to the rue Mazarin, where, as soon as she was alone with Joseph, Agathe fell into gloomy silence.

The following Sunday Philippe came again. This time his mother was visibly present at the sitting. She served breakfast and questioned the dragoon, learning that the nephew of old Madame Hochon, her mother’s friend, played a considerable part in literature. Philippe and his friend Giroudeau lived among journalists, actresses, and booksellers, regarded as cashiers. Philippe, who had been drinking kirsch before posing, was loquacious and boasted he was about to become a great man. But when Joseph asked about his resources he was silent. There was no newspaper the next day, it being a fete, and to finish the portrait Philippe proposed to sit again the morrow. Joseph said the Salon was near and he lacked money for two frames; he must finish a copy of a Rubens ordered by Elie Magus, a dealer; the original belonged to a wealthy Swiss banker who had lent it for ten days, and the next day was the last; the sitting must be put off till the following Sunday.

“Is that it?” asked Philippe, pointing to a copy of Rubens on an easel.

“Yes,” replied Joseph; “it is worth twenty thousand francs. That’s what genius can do. It will take me all tomorrow to get the tones of the original and make the copy look so old it can’t be distinguished from it.”

“Adieu, mother,” said Philippe, kissing Agathe. “Next Sunday, then.”

The next day Elie Magus was to come for his copy. Joseph’s friend Pierre Grassou, working for the same dealer, wanted to see it when finished. To play a trick, Joseph, when he heard his knock, put the copy, varnished with a glaze of his own, in place of the original, and put the original on his easel. Pierre Grassou was completely taken in and then amazed and delighted at Joseph’s success.

“Do you think it will deceive old Magus?” he said.

“We shall see,” answered the latter.

The dealer did not come as promised. It was getting late; Agathe dined that day with Madame Desroches, who had recently lost her husband, and Joseph proposed to Pierre Grassou to dine at his table d’hote. As he went out he left the key of his studio with the concierge.

An hour later Philippe appeared and said to the concierge,—

“I am to sit this evening; Joseph will be in soon, and I will wait for him in the studio.”

The woman gave him the key; Philippe went upstairs, took the copy thinking it was the original, went down again, returned the key saying he had forgotten something, and hurried off to sell his Rubens for three thousand francs. He had sent a message to Elie Magus asking him not to call till the following day.

That evening when Joseph returned, bringing his mother from Madame Desroches’s, the concierge told him of Philippe’s trick—how he had called intending to wait and gone away immediately.

“I am ruined—unless he has had the delicacy to take the copy,” cried Joseph, instantly suspecting theft. He ran up three flights and rushed into his studio. “God be praised!” he ejaculated. “He is, what he always has been, a vile scoundrel.”

Agathe, who had followed Joseph, did not understand his words; but when he explained, she stood still with tears in her eyes.

“Have I but one son?” she said in a broken voice.

“We have never yet degraded him to the eyes of strangers,” said Joseph; “but we must warn the concierge. We shall keep the keys ourselves. I’ll finish his blackguard face from memory; there’s not much to do.”

“Leave it as it is; it will pain me too much ever to look at it,” answered the mother, heart-stricken at such wickedness.

Philippe had been told how the money for this copy was to be expended; he knew the abyss into which he would plunge his brother through the loss of the Rubens; yet nothing restrained him. After this crime Agathe never mentioned him; her face acquired cold, concentrated, bitter despair; one thought possessed her.

“Some day,” she said to herself, “we shall hear of a Bridau in the police courts.”

Two months later, as Agathe was about to start for her office, an old officer announcing himself as a friend of Philippe’s on urgent business called on Madame Bridau, who happened to be in Joseph’s studio.

When Giroudeau gave his name, mother and son trembled, and none the less because the ex-dragoon had the face of a tough old sailor of the worst type. His fishy gray eyes, piebald moustache, the remains of shaggy hair fringing a skull the color of fresh butter, all gave a debauched and libidinous expression. He wore an old iron-gray overcoat decorated with the red ribbon of a Legion of honor officer, which met with difficulty over a gastronomic stomach and a mouth that stretched from ear to ear, and a pair of powerful shoulders. The torso was supported by spindling legs, while rubicund tints on the cheek-bones testified to a rollicking life. Deep wrinkles overhung a velvet collar worse for wear. Among other adornments, the ex-dragoon wore enormous gold rings in his ears.

“What a ‘noceur’!” thought Joseph, using a popular expression meaning a loose fish.

“Madame,” said Finot’s uncle and cashier, “your son is in so unfortunate a position that his friends find it necessary to ask you to share the somewhat heavy expense he is to them. He can no longer work at the office; and Mademoiselle Florentine, of the Porte-Saint-Martin, has taken him to lodge with her, in a miserable attic in the rue de Vendome. Philippe is dying; and if you and his brother are not able to pay for the doctor and medicines, we shall have to have him taken to the hospital of the Capuchins. For three hundred francs we would keep him where he is. But he must have a nurse; at night, when Florentine is at the theatre, he persists in going out and takes things injurious to his treatment. As we are fond of him, this makes us unhappy. The poor fellow has pledged the pension of his cross for three years; he is temporarily displaced from his office, and has literally nothing. He will kill himself, madame, unless we put him into Doctor Dubois’s private asylum. It is a decent hospital; they will take him for ten francs a day. Florentine and I will pay half, if you will pay the rest; it won’t be more than two months.”

“Monsieur, it is difficult for a mother not to be eternally grateful for your kindness to her son,” replied Agathe; “but this son is banished from my heart, and as for money, I have none. Not to be a burden on my son who works day and night and deserves all a mother’s love, I am assistant in a lottery-office—at my age!”

“And you, young man,” said the old dragoon to Joseph; “can’t you do as much for your brother as a poor dancer and an old soldier?”

“Look here!” said Joseph, out of patience; “do you want me to tell you in artist language what I think of your visit? Well, you have come to swindle us on false pretences.”

“To-morrow your brother shall go to the hospital.”

“And he will do very well there,” answered Joseph. “If I were in like case, I should go there too.”

Giroudeau withdrew, much disappointed and mortified at being obliged to send to a hospital a man who had carried the Emperor’s orders at Montereau. Three months later, at the end of July, as Agathe one morning was crossing the Pont Neuf to avoid paying a sou at the Pont des Arts, she saw, along the Quai de l’Ecole, a man bearing all the signs of second-class poverty, who she thought resembled Philippe. In Paris there are three classes of poverty. First, the poverty of those who preserve appearances and have a future—young men, artists, momentarily unfortunate—whose distress is not visible except to a close observer; they continue to drive in cabriolets. Second, the poverty of old men indifferent to everything—the poverty of small incomes, old clerks wearing alpaca overcoats with the cross of the Legion. Third, poverty in rags, the people’s poverty, poetic and often depicted in art. The man Agathe thought her son rode astride the last two classes: ragged neck-cloth, scurfy hat, broken patched boots, threadbare coat whose buttons had shed their mould, torn pockets and dirty collar. Scraps of flue were in the creases; his hands were black as a mechanic’s. A knitted waistcoat, discolored, showed below the sleeves and above the trousers, likely instead of a shirt. Philippe wore a green silk shade with a wire edge over his eyes; his nearly bald head, his skin tints, and his sunken face too plainly revealed he had just left the terrible Hopital du Midi. His blue overcoat, whitened at the seams, was still decorated with the ribbon of his cross; passers-by looked at the hero—some victim of government—with curiosity and commiseration; the rosette attracted notice, and the fiercest ultras were jealous for the Order’s honor. In those days, however much the government tried to discredit the Order by bestowing crosses right and left, there were not fifty-three thousand persons decorated.

Agathe trembled through her being. If it were impossible to love this son any longer, she could still suffer for him. Quivering with motherhood, she wept as she saw the brilliant staff officer of the Emperor turn to enter a tobacconist’s and pause on the threshold; he had felt in his pocket and found nothing. Agathe left the bridge, crossed the quay rapidly, took out her purse, thrust it into Philippe’s hand, and fled as if she had committed a crime. After that she ate nothing for two days; before her was the horrible vision of her son dying of hunger in Paris.

“When he has spent all the money in my purse, who will give him any?” she thought. “Giroudeau did not deceive us; Philippe is just out of that hospital.”

She no longer saw the assassin of her aunt, the scourge of the family, the domestic thief, gambler, drunkard, low liver of a bad life; she saw only a man recovering from illness yet doomed to die of starvation, the smoker deprived of tobacco. At forty-seven she grew to look seventy. Her eyes were dimmed with tears and prayers. Yet it was not the last grief this son was to bring; her worst fears were destined to be realized. A conspiracy of officers was discovered in the army, and Moniteur articles detailing arrests were hawked about the streets.

In the depths of her cage in the rue Vivienne lottery-office, Agathe heard the name of Philippe Bridau. She fainted, and the manager, understanding her trouble and the necessity of action, gave her two weeks’ leave.

“Ah! my friend,” she said to Joseph as she went to bed, “it is our severity which drove him to it.”

“I’ll go and see Desroches,” answered Joseph.

While the artist confided his brother’s affairs to the younger Desroches—who by now had the reputation of a keen and astute lawyer and did sundry services for personages of distinction, among others for des Lupeaulx—Giroudeau called upon the widow. This time Agathe believed him.

“Madame,” he said, “if you can produce twelve thousand francs your son will be set at liberty for want of proof. It is necessary to buy the silence of two witnesses.”

“I will get the money,” said the poor mother, without knowing how.

Inspired by this danger, she wrote to her godmother, old Madame Hochon, begging her to ask Jean-Jacques Rouget to send the twelve thousand francs and save his nephew. If Rouget refused, she entreated Madame Hochon to lend them, promising to return them in two years. By return courier she received the following letter:—

My dear girl: Though your brother has an income of not less than forty thousand francs a year, without counting sums he has laid by for seventeen years, which Monsieur Hochon estimates at more than six hundred thousand francs, he will not give a penny to nephews he has never seen. As for me, you know I cannot dispose of a farthing while my husband lives. Hochon is the greatest miser in Issoudun. I do not know what he does with his money; he does not give twenty francs a year to his grandchildren. As for borrowing the money, I should have to get his signature, and he would refuse it. I have not even attempted to speak to your brother, who lives with a concubine, to whom he is a slave. It is pitiable to see how the poor man is treated at home, when he might have a sister and nephews to take care of him.

I have hinted that your presence at Issoudun might save your brother and rescue a fortune of forty, perhaps sixty, thousand francs a year from the claws of that slut; but you either do not answer me, or you seem never to understand my meaning. So today I write without epistolary circumlocution. I feel for your misfortune, but, my dearest, I can do no more than pity you. And this is why: Hochon, at eighty-five, takes four meals a day, eats a salad with hard-boiled eggs every night, and frisk about like a rabbit. I shall have spent my whole life—for he will live to write my epitaph—without ever having had twenty francs in my purse. If you will come to Issoudun and counteract the influence of that concubine over your brother, you must stay with me, for there are reasons why Rouget cannot receive you in his house; but even then, I shall have hard work to get my husband to let me have you here. However, you can safely come; I can make him mind me. I know a way to get what I want out of him; I have only to speak of making my will. It seems such a horrid thing that I do not often have recourse to it; but for you, dear Agathe, I will do the impossible.

I hope your Philippe will get out of his trouble; I beg you to employ a good lawyer. In any case, come to Issoudun as soon as you can. Remember that your imbecile of a brother at fifty-seven is an older and weaker man than Monsieur Hochon. So it is pressing. People are talking of a will that cuts off your inheritance; Monsieur Hochon says there is still time to get it revoked.

Adieu, my little Agathe; may God help you! Believe in the love of your godmother,

Maximilienne Hochon, nee Lousteau.

P.S. Has my nephew, Etienne, who writes in the newspapers and is intimate, they tell me, with your son Philippe, been to pay his respects to you? But come at once to Issoudun, and we will talk.

This letter greatly impressed Agathe, who showed it to Joseph after mentioning Giroudeau’s proposal. The artist, wary when it concerned his brother, pointed out she ought to tell everything to Desroches.

Conscious of that wisdom, Agathe went with her son the next morning, at six, to find Desroches in the rue de Bussy. The lawyer, cold and stern as his late father, with a sharp voice, rough skin, implacable eyes, and a fox-like visage, bounded like a tiger when he heard of Giroudeau’s visit and proposal.

“And pray, mere Bridau,” he cried in his cracked voice, “how long will you be duped by your cursed brigand of a son? Don’t give him a farthing. Make yourself easy, I’ll answer for Philippe. I should like to see him brought before the Court of Peers; it might save his future. You are afraid he will be condemned; but I say, may it please God his lawyer lets him be convicted. Go to Issoudun, secure the property for your children. If you don’t succeed, if your brother has made a will in favor of that—woman, and you can’t make him revoke it—get all the evidence you can of undue influence, and I’ll institute proceedings. But you are too honest a woman to know how to get at such facts. I’ll go myself to Issoudun in the holidays—if I can.”

That “go myself” made Joseph tremble. Desroches winked at him to let his mother go downstairs first, and then detained the young man for a moment.

“Your brother is a great scoundrel; he is the cause of the discovery of this conspiracy—intentionally or not, I can’t say, for the rascal is so sly nobody can know the exact truth. Fool or traitor—take your choice. He will be put under police surveillance, nothing more. You needn’t be uneasy; no one knows this secret but myself. Go to Issoudun with your mother. You have good sense; try to save the property.”

“Come, my poor mother, Desroches is right,” said Joseph, rejoining Agathe on the staircase. “I have sold my two pictures, let us start for Berry; you have two weeks’ leave of absence.”

After writing to her godmother announcing their arrival, Agathe and Joseph started the next evening for Issoudun, leaving Philippe to his fate. The diligence rolled through the rue d’Enfer toward the Orleans highroad. When Agathe saw the Luxembourg, to which Philippe had been transferred, she could not refrain from saying,—

“If it were not for the Allies he would never be there!”

Many sons would have smiled with pity; the artist, alone with his mother in the coupe, caught her in his arms and pressed her to his heart, exclaiming,—

“Oh, mother! you are a mother just as Raphael was a painter. And you will always be a fool of a mother!”

Madame Bridau’s mind, soon diverted from her griefs by the journey’s distractions, began to dwell on its purpose. She re-read Madame Hochon’s letter, struck by “concubine” and “slut” used by a septuagenarian as pious and respectable as she, and by “imbecile” applied to Rouget; she wondered how her presence at Issoudun would save the inheritance. Joseph, poor disinterested artist that he was, knew little about the Code, and his mother’s remark absorbed him.

“Before our friend Desroches sent us off to protect our rights, he ought to have explained the means of doing so,” he exclaimed.

“So far as my poor head, which whirls at the thought of Philippe in prison—without tobacco, perhaps, and about to appear before the Court of Peers—leaves me any distinct memory,” returned Agathe, “I think young Desroches said we were to get evidence of undue influence, in case my brother has made a will in favor of that—that—woman.”

“He is good at that, Desroches is,” cried the painter. “Bah! if we can make nothing of it I’ll get him to come himself.”

“Well, don’t let us trouble our heads uselessly,” said Agathe. “When we get to Issoudun my godmother will tell us what to do.”

This conversation, just after they changed coaches at Orleans and entered the Sologne, shows how ill fitted the painter and his mother were for the task Desroches had set them.

Returning to Issoudun after thirty years’ absence, Agathe was about to find changes in its manners that make it necessary to sketch a picture of the town. Without it the reader would scarcely understand Madame Hochon’s heroism in assisting her goddaughter, or the strange situation of Jean-Jacques Rouget. Though Doctor Rouget had taught his son to regard Agathe as a stranger, it was extraordinary that for thirty years a brother should have given no sign of life to a sister. Such silence was plainly caused by peculiar circumstances, and any other sister and nephew would long ago have inquired. There is, moreover, a certain connection between Issoudun’s condition and the Bridau family’s interests, which will appear as the story goes on.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
CHAPTER VII

Issoudun, without offence to Paris, is one of the oldest cities in France. Despite the tradition making Probus the Noah of the Gauls, Caesar praises the excellent wine of Champ-Fort (“de Campo Forti”), still among Issoudun’s best vintages. Rigord describes the city’s great population and commerce. Yet these testimonies underestimate its true antiquity. Excavations by the local archaeologist Monsieur Armand Peremet uncovered, beneath the celebrated tower of Issoudun, a fifth-century basilica, probably the only one in France. Its stones came from a Roman temple that stood on the same site, preserving in its materials the sign-manual of an earlier civilization.

According to Peremet, like other French towns whose names end in “-dun” (“dunum”), Issoudun bears evidence of an autochthonous origin. “Dun,” associated with Druidical worship, shows a Celtic religious and military settlement. Beneath the Gaulish dun likely lay a Roman temple to Isis, whence, says Chaumon, the name Issous-Dun—“Is” being an abbreviation of “Isis.” Richard Coeur-de-lion doubtless built the famous tower (where he coined money) above the fifth-century basilica; he used the church as a foundation and preserved it by wrapping it in feudal fortifications. Issoudun was then the seat of the short-lived power of the Routiers and Cottereaux, adventurers Henry II. sent against his son Richard during his rebellion as Count of Poitou.

The history of Aquitaine, unwritten by the Benedictines, will probably remain obscure, for there are no more Benedictines to illuminate those archaeological tenebrae. Another sign of Issoudun’s ancient importance is the conversion of the Tournemine into a canal, a little stream elevated above the level of the Theols that surrounds the town—undoubtedly Roman engineering. Moreover, a suburb extending north from the castle is traversed by a street long called the rue de Rome, and its inhabitants, with distinct racial features, call themselves Romans. Mostly vine-growers, they display a stubbornness of manners and customs from their origin—and perhaps from their victory over the Cottereaux and Routiers, whom they exterminated on the plain of Charost in the twelfth century.

After the 1830 insurrection, France was too agitated to note the uprising of Issoudun’s vine-growers; a terrible affair there has remained unpublished for good reasons. The bourgeois of Issoudun refused the military entry, following medieval practice of declaring themselves responsible for their city. The government yielded to this sturdy people backed by seven or eight thousand vine-growers, who had burned archives and the offices of indirect taxation and dragged a customs officer through the streets, shouting at every lantern, “Let us hang him here!” The national guard saved the man’s life by taking him to prison under the pretext of drawing up an indictment. The general entered town only after a compromise with the vine-growers; it took courage to show himself. When he appeared at the hotel-de-ville, a man from the faubourg de Rome swung a volant—a huge pruning-hook on a pole—round his neck, crying, “No more clerks, or there’s an end to compromise!” The man would have been killed, had not one leader of the revolt intervened, having been promised that the chambers should be asked to suppress the excisemen.

In the fourteenth century Issoudun still had sixteen or seventeen thousand inhabitants, remnants of a population double that number in Rigord’s time. Charles VII owned a mansion still known into the eighteenth century as the Maison du Roi. Once a center of the woollen trade, the town supplied much of Europe with wool, making blankets, hats, and Chevreautin gloves. Under Louis XIV., Issoudun, birthplace of Baron and Bourdaloue, was noted for elegance and correct speech. The curate Poupard remarks on Issoudun’s inhabitants’ subtlety and wit. Today both wit and splendor have faded. Issoudun, whose area shows its ancient importance, now has barely twelve thousand inhabitants, including vine-dressers of four large suburbs—Saint-Paterne, Vilatte, Rome, and Alouette—which are almost small towns. The bourgeoisie are spread through the streets. Issoudun still holds the market for Berry’s fleeces, a commerce threatened by improvements in stock breeding being introduced everywhere except in Berry.

Issoudun’s vineyards produce a wine drunk through two departments and, if made like Burgundy or Gascony, could be among France’s best. But adherence to “doing as our fathers did” prevails. Vine-growers leave the grape refuse in the juice during fermentation, which makes the wine detestable though it could be a source of wealth. They say the refuse’s bitterness lessens with age, so a vintage will keep a century—an excuse even Guillaume le Breton gives in his “Phillippide.”

Issoudun’s decline stems from this sluggishness sunk to torpor, exemplified by one fact. When a highroad between Paris and Toulouse was discussed, the natural line from Vierzon to Chateauroux would pass through Issoudun, shorter than the present route via Vatan. But the leading people and city council demanded it go by Vatan, arguing that a road through their town would raise provisions and force them to pay thirty sous for a chicken. The only parallel is in wild Sardinia: when Charles Albert planned a highway between Sassari and Cagliari, the direct line through lawless Bornova was opposed; the first engineer was killed, and the road made a circuit eight miles longer.

The continual lowering of wine prices, though it pleases Issoudun’s bourgeoisie seeking cheap provisions, threatens the vine-growers with ruin as costs and taxes weigh heavier—just as the woollen trade suffers from lack of improvement in sheep breeding. Country folk fear change, even that most beneficial. A Parisian may show a laborer that eating some meat would be cheaper and strengthen him, but the Berrichon, fearing gossip and being pointed at, refuses it. “Think of the gossip, monsieur,” they say, preferring to endure poverty rather than be talked about. Many bourgeois utter the phrase with inward pride.

While ignorance and custom are invincible in the country, the town itself has reached social stagnation. Meeting decaying fortunes by sordid economy, each family lives for itself. Society lacks the class distinctions that give manners character. There are no nobles—Cottereaux, Routiers, Jacquerie, religious wars, and the Revolution abolished them, a triumph Issoudun applauds. Issoudun repeatedly refused a garrison, claiming cheap provisions, thus losing intercourse with the age and profits from troops. Before 1756 it was a delightful garrison town. A judicial feud between a lieutenant-general and the Marquis de Chapt, whose son—a dragoon officer—was put to death, deprived the town of a garrison. The forty-fourth demi-brigade’s sojourn during the civil war did not reconcile inhabitants to soldiers.

Bourges, with a declining population, suffers the same malady. Vitality leaves these communities. The government is to blame: an administration should find and heal wounds on the body politic by sending energetic men with power to change things. Instead, it approves this fatal tranquillity. Who would accept being buried in such arrondissements, where useful work lacks glory? Any ambitious outsider soon falls into local inertia. Issoudun would have benumbed Napoleon.

In 1822 the arrondissement’s administrators all belonged to Berry. Administration became nullity or farce—except in rare important cases. The procureur du roi, Monsieur Mouilleron, was cousin to the whole community, his substitute from a town family. The court judge, before attaining his seat, became famous for a provincial saying that branded him forever: ending a summing-up, he told the accused, “My poor Pierre! the thing is as plain as day; your head will be cut off. Let this be a lesson to you.” The commissary of police, in office since the Restoration, had relations throughout the arrondissement. Religion’s influence was null; even the curate was held in little esteem.

This radical, ignorant bourgeoisie—loving to annoy others—spread tales, some comic, about Jean-Jacques Rouget and his servant-woman. Still, children went to Sunday-school, schools were kept up, mass said, taxes paid (the sole thing Paris extracts from the provinces), and the mayor passed resolutions. But these acts were routine, so the laxity of local government matched the moral and intellectual state of the governed. Subsequent events will show effects of this condition, common in many provinces, especially the South. The state to which bourgeois ascendency has reduced Issoudun may spread across France, even to Paris, if the bourgeois continue to rule domestic and foreign policy.

A word of topography. Issoudun stretches north-south along a hillside rounding toward the Chateauroux road. At the hill’s foot a canal, now called the “Riviere forcee,” fed from the Theols, was built when the town flourished for manufactories or to flood rampart moats. The “Riviere forcee” is an artificial arm of the Tournemine, joining other streams beyond the Rome suburb. These threads of water and the rivers irrigate wide meadows, enclosed by yellowish or white terraces dotted with black speckles—the vineyards’ aspect for seven months. Vine-growers cut plants annually to ugly stumps, unsupported, sheltered by barrels. A traveller from Vierzon, Vatan, or Chateauroux, weary of monotony, is pleasantly surprised by Issoudun’s meadows—an oasis supplying vegetables across thirty miles. Below the Rome suburb lies a vast tract of kitchen-gardens, upper and lower Baltan. A long poplar avenue leads from town across the meadows to the ancient convent of Frapesle, whose English gardens, ambitiously named Tivoli, are a Sunday rendezvous for lovers.

Traces of ancient grandeur reveal themselves; the most suggestive are the town’s divisions. The chateau, once almost a town with walls and moats, is a distinct quarter entered even now through ancient gateways by three bridges across two river arms, with the appearance of an old city. Ramparts show formidable foundations on which houses have sprung. Above the chateau is the famous tower, once the citadel. Conquering the city, which lay around these two fortified points, still left the tower and castle to be won.

The Saint-Paterne suburb, a palette-shaped extension beyond the tower encroaching on meadows, was probably part of the ancient city. In 1822 this opinion seemed certain because of the then-existence of the charming church of Saint-Paterne, recently pulled down by the heir of the buyer from the nation. This Romanesque church, one of France’s finest, perished without a single drawing of its portal, which was perfectly preserved. The lone voice raised to save it found no echo in town or department. Though the castle appears like an old town with narrow streets and mansions, the main city, repeatedly captured and burned—especially during the Fronde—has a modern air. Spacious streets and well-built houses contrast with the citadel’s aspect, earning Issoudun, in some geographies, the epithet “pretty.”

In such a town, lacking activity, taste for art, or learned occupations, and where everyone stayed within a narrow circle, many young men after the Restoration in 1816 had no career until marriage or inheritance. Bored at home and lacking distraction, they “shed their cuticle” by sowing wild oats at the town’s expense. Avoiding daytime escapades to prevent recognition and arraignment before police courts, they chose the night. Thus, amid traces of lost civilizations, a last flare of drollery burst into being.

These young men amused themselves as courts and soldiers once did, banding together for mutual help and tricks. They became an organized conspiracy—the “Knights of Idleness.” By day they played saints, sleeping late after nights of mischief. The Knights began with commonplace pranks—unhooking and changing signs, ringing bells, flinging casks into neighbors’ cellars, rousing inhabitants with crashes suggesting explosions. In Issoudun, cellars are entered by openings near the door, covered by a wooden scuttle with iron hinges and padlock.

In 1816 such provincial pranks persisted. But in 1817 the Order acquired a Grand Master and, until 1823, Maxence Gilet, called Max, led mischief that spread something like terror in Issoudun and kept artisans and bourgeois perpetually uneasy.

Maxence Gilet’s antecedents, youth, and vigor predestined him for the role. The town supposed him the natural son of the sub-delegate Lousteau—the brother of Madame Hochon whose gallantries were remembered—and some said he might be Doctor Rouget’s son; in truth his father was a dragoon officer garrisoned at Bourges. Because Rouget and Lousteau had been close friends, both claimed paternity, leaving the matter undecided. Max’s mother, wife of a poor sabot-maker in the Rome suburb, was marvellously beautiful—a Trasteverine beauty she transmitted to her son. Madame Gilet, pregnant with Max in 1788, enjoyed the town’s attribution of his paternity to the two friends, perhaps to set them against each other. Gilet, the old drunkard, colluded with his wife; she kept the reputed fathers ignorant to secure protectors for her son. In Paris she would have prospered; at Issoudun she lived sometimes comfortably, mostly wretchedly, and in the long run was despised. Madame Hochon paid sixty francs a year for the lad’s schooling—a liberality attributed to her brother living at Sancerre.

Doctor Rouget, unlucky in sons, seeing Max’s beauty, paid the youngster’s seminary board until 1805. Lousteau died in 1800, and the doctor continued payments, leaving paternity forever unresolved. Maxence, the butt of jests, was soon forgotten because in 1806, a year after Rouget’s death, he began a venturesome life. Gifted with strength and agility, he plotted with Monsieur Hochon’s grandsons to vex the grocers, gathered fruit before owners could pick it, scaled walls, excelled at physical exercises, and loved hunting. Time was spent firing at a mark rather than studying, and he spent the doctor’s money on powder and shot for a pistol old Gilet had given him. In autumn 1806, at seventeen, Max committed an involuntary homicide: startling a pregnant woman in dusk while stealing fruit, her death was blamed on him. Threatened with the guillotine by Gilet, Max fled to Bourges, enlisted with a regiment bound for Egypt, and nothing came of the woman’s death.

In three campaigns Max distinguished himself and rose to captain; his lack of formal education did not impede him. In Portugal, 1809, he was left for dead in an English battery entered but not held. Taken prisoner, he was sent to the Spanish hulks at Cabrera, the most horrible prison station. Friends petitioned for the Legion of Honor and major’s rank, but the Emperor, campaigning in Austria and displeased with Portuguese events, withheld favors from those taken prisoner. Max was at Cabrera from 1810 to 1814. There he was demoralized: the hulks were like galleys, full of crime and infamy. To maintain will and protect against corruption, he killed in duels seven bullies among his fellow-prisoners, ridding the island of tyrants. He then reigned in his hulk by skill with weapons, strength, and cleverness.

But he committed arbitrary acts; some currying favor became his minions. In that school of misery, bitter minds dreamed of vengeance and hatched sophistries tending to evil. Though depraved, Max returned innocent when peace came in April 1814. He found his parents dead in the almshouse and destitute. At Napoleon’s landing at Cannes he went to Paris to ask for rank and decoration. A marshal then minister of war, recalling Max’s conduct in Portugal, put him in the Guard as captain—giving him the grade of major in infantry—but could not secure the cross. “The Emperor says that you will know how to win it at the first chance,” said the marshal. Indeed, Napoleon put Max on the decoration list the evening after Fleurus, where he distinguished himself.

After Waterloo Max retreated to the Loire. At disbandment Marshal Feltre refused to recognize his grade or cross. Enraged, Max declared he would not serve without rank or cross. The war-office deemed his demands presumptuous; he resigned, losing half-pay. Issoudun was roused at seeing the brave young man left with only twenty napoleons; the mayor gave him a six-hundred-franc office, which Max held a few months before giving it up. Captain Carpentier, a faithful Napoleonic officer, replaced him.

By now Max was grand master of the Knights of Idleness and lived in ways that lost him the bourgeoisie’s goodwill, though none openly told him, for he was feared, even by officers who, like him, had refused to serve the Bourbons and returned to Berry. Issoudun, small and unimportant, contained more Bonapartists than any town in France; these men became, as is known, nearly all Liberals.

In Issoudun and vicinity a dozen officers shared Max’s position. They admired and followed him, except Captain Carpentier and Major Mignonnet. Carpentier, a cavalry officer risen from the ranks, had married into the town’s best family; Mignonnet, from the École Polytechnique, served in a corps that held itself superior. In the Imperial armies two soldierly shades existed: a majority who despised civilians, and others—particularly artillerymen—more republican and not adopting the military-civil dichotomy. Thus Major Potel and Captain Renard befriended Max, while Mignonnet and Carpentier sided with the bourgeoisie, judging his conduct dishonorable.

Major Mignonnet, lean and dignified, busied himself with steam-engine problems and lived modestly with Monsieur and Madame Carpentier. His gentle manners and scientific pursuits won town respect; he and Carpentier were often said to be “quite another thing” from Potel, Renard, Max, and the cafe Militaire habitués, who retained soldierly manners and the Empire’s defective morals.

When Madame Bridau returned to Issoudun, Max was excluded from society. He showed self-respect by not appearing at the club or complaining about the reprobation, though he was the town’s handsomest, best-dressed man, spent much money, and kept a horse—a rarity at Issoudun as Byron’s horse in Venice. We shall see how Max, poor and apparently without means, became the town’s dandy. His shameful conduct, earning bourgeois and religious contempt, connected with interests that brought Agathe and Joseph to Issoudun.

Judging by his audacious bearing and face, Max cared little for public opinion; he expected revenge and to lord it over those who condemned him. Although the bourgeoisie thought ill of him, the common people admired his courage, daring, and decision—his degradations were mostly unknown, even to the bourgeoisie. Max played a role like the blacksmith in the Fair Maid of Perth: champion of Bonapartism and Opposition, counted on in great occasions. One incident clarifies his heroism.

In 1819 a battalion commanded by royalist officers, fresh from the Maison Rouge, passed through Issoudun on its way to Bourges garrison. Uncertain what to do in so constitutional a place, they lingered at the cafe Militaire. Every provincial town has such a cafe; Issoudun’s, at the place d’Armes on the rampart corner, kept by a widow of an officer, was the rendezvous of Bonapartists—officers on half-pay and others idolizing the Emperor. Since 1816 a banquet commemorated Napoleon’s coronation. The three royalists first in asked for papers including the “Quotidienne” and “Drapeau Blanc.” Issoudun’s cafe politics did not allow such royalist journals; the establishment stocked the “Commerce”—a name the “Constitutionnel” adopted for a time after suppression, and locals called it the “Constitutionel,” understanding the play of words.

The fat landlady said she did not take those papers. “What papers do you take then?” asked a captain. The waiter brought the “Commerce.”

“Is that your paper? Have you no other?”

“No,” said the waiter.

The captain tore it up, flung the pieces on the floor, spat on them, and cried, “Bring dominos!”

Word of this insult to the Constitutional party spread through town. One phrase was on every lip—“Let us tell Max!” Max soon heard. The royalists still played dominoes when he entered the cafe with Major Potel and Captain Renard, followed by at least thirty young men curious for the outcome, most remaining outside. The room filled.

“Waiter, my newspaper,” said Max quietly.

A little comedy followed. The fat hostess, timid and conciliatory, said, “Captain, I have lent it!”

“Send for it,” cried one of Max’s friends.

“Can’t you do without it?” said the waiter; “we have not got it.”

The royalists laughed.

“They have torn it up!” cried a youth, looking at the royalist captain’s feet.

“Who dared destroy that paper?” demanded Max, rising, eyes flashing, arms crossed.

“And we spat upon it,” replied the three officers.

“You have insulted the whole town!” said Max, turning livid.

“Well, what of that?” asked the youngest officer.

With dexterity and audacity unexpected by the young men, Max slapped the nearest officer, saying, “Do you understand French?”

They fought in the allée de Frapesle, three against three; Potel and Renard would not let Max fight alone. Max killed his man. Major Potel wounded another so severely the young officer died in hospital the next day. The third escaped with a sword cut after wounding Captain Renard. The battalion left for Bourges that night. The affair spread through Berry and firmly established Max as a hero.

The Knights of Idleness, all young—the eldest not more than twenty-five—admired Max. Some envied his position. Under such a leader they accomplished great mischief. After May 1817, never a week passed without the town’s being thrown into uproar. Max imposed conditions; statutes were drawn. These youths trained like pupils of Amoros—bold, agile, clever and strong as criminals. They practised roof-climbing, scaling houses, noiseless walking, mixing mortar, walling up doors, and collected ropes, ladders, tools, and disguises. They perfected invention and execution of pranks, achieving a Panurge-like genius for evil that provoked laughter while covering victims with ridicule, silencing complaints. Being sons of good families, they had household information facilitating outrages.

Sometimes they lay in ambush along the Grand’rue or the Basse rue, towns’ arteries. Crouched in wall angles at the hour households slept, they called along the town in tones of terror: “What’s the matter?” “What is it?” repeated cries woke citizens who hurried out in shirts and caps with lights, asking questions and showing queer faces.

A poor old bookbinder who believed in hobgoblins worked in a small basement shop. The Knights, disguised as devils, invaded at night, put him in his cutting-press, and left him shrieking as if among hell's souls. He roused the neighbors with tales of Lucifer and was nearly driven insane.

In a severe winter the Knights took down the collector of taxes’ chimney and rebuilt it overnight so noiselessly that they left no trace, but arranged the flue to send all smoke into the house. The collector suffered two months before discovering the cause and had to rebuild a new chimney.

Another time they put three trusses of hay dusted with brimstone and oiled paper down the chimney of a pious old woman friend of Madame Hochon. In the morning, lighting her fire, she thought she had started a volcano. Fire-engines came, the population rushed to help; Knights among the firemen deluged her house, frightening her as much with water as they had with the “fire.” She grew ill with fear.

To keep someone on guard and in terror, they wrote anonymous letters warning of an impending robbery, then stole softly around his house or under his windows, whistling as if to call each other.

A famous performance was sending letters to all heirs of a miserly old woman likely to leave large property, announcing her death and requesting them to be present when the seals were affixed. Eighty persons arrived from neighboring towns in deep mourning—widows, sons, children, some in carrioles or carts. Imagine the scene between servants and arrivals and the notaries’ consultations; it created a sort of riot.

At last the sub-prefect waked to the intolerable state, made worse by the difficulty of discovering perpetrators. Suspicion fell on several youths; but the National Guard was a mere name, there was no garrison, and the lieutenant of police had only eight gendarmes—no patrols—so proof was impossible. The sub-prefect was put in the “order of the night” and became fair game. He breakfasted on two fresh eggs, kept chickens, boiled eggs himself with watch in hand, boasting his egg-boiling skill. For two years he had boiled eggs successfully, earning many witticisms. Then, for a month, each night his eggs were taken and replaced by hard-boiled ones, ruining his reputation as the “sous-prefet à l’oeuf.” He lost his egg-boiling laurels and never suspected the Knights, who had planned instead. Max greased the sub-prefect’s stoves nightly with fetid oil, making the house unbearable. His wife, going to mass one morning, found her shawl glued inside by a tenacious substance and had to go without it. The sub-prefect finally requested transfer. His cowardly submissiveness helped establish the Knights’ weird and comic authority.

Beyond the rue des Minimes and the place Misere lay a quarter enclosed between an arm of the “Riviere forcee” and the ramparts, from the place d’Armes to the pottery market. This square was filled with poor houses crowded together, divided by streets so narrow two abreast could not pass. This quarter, a sort of cour des Miracles, housed poor people and lowly trades—hovels and “blind houses.” It has been, from earliest ages, an accursed quarter, haunt of evil-doers; one thoroughfare is called “the street of the Executioner.” For five centuries it’s been customary for the executioner to have a red door. The executioner of Chateauroux’s assistant is said to live there, seen rarely; vine-dressers alone keep intercourse with him, for he inherits the craft of curing wounds and fractures. In Issoudun’s heyday women wandered this quarter. It was home to second-hand sellers, old-clothes dealers whose wares infected the air; in short, the rendezvous of that apocryphal population found in such city parts, where two or three Jews sometimes gain ascendancy.

At a corner in the livelier half of the quarter existed, from 1815 to 1823 and perhaps later, a public-house kept by a woman called Mère Cognette. The house was well built of white stone with ashlar and cement infill, one storey with an attic; over the door hung a huge pine branch like a Florentine bronze. A blue poster showed, above the words “Good Beer of Mars,” a soldier pouring foam in an arched jet toward a very décolletée woman—an image to make Delacroix swoon.

The ground floor was an immense hall serving as kitchen and dining-room, from whose beams provisions hung on huge nails. A winding staircase led to the upper storey; at the foot a door opened into a low, long room lit from a narrow, sunken provincial court like a chimney flue. Hidden by a shed and walls, this low room was where the Bad Boys held their plenary court. Ostensibly Pere Cognet boarded country folk on market-days; secretly he was landlord to the Knights. Pere Cognet, formerly a groom in a rich household, had married La Cognette, a cook in a good family. In the Rome suburb the town follows the Latin custom of placing a feminine termination on the husband’s name and giving it to the wife.

By uniting savings Pere Cognet and his spouse bought their house. La Cognette, forty, tall and plump, with a Roxelane nose, swarthy skin, jet-black hair, lively brown eyes and a mirthful intelligence, was chosen by Max as the Order’s Leonarde for her character and cookery. Pere Cognet, about fifty-six, thick-set and under his wife’s rule—“he only sees things with a good eye,” she joked, being blind of the other—kept absolute silence about nightly gatherings. From 1816 to 1823 neither revealed what went on. Their devotion was absolute, but self-interest secured their affection and silence. Whenever the Knights knocked a certain way Pere Cognet would get up, light the fire and candles, open the door, and fetch a particular wine laid in for the Order; La Cognette prepared an excellent supper, eaten before or after expeditions planned the previous evening or day.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
CHAPTER VIII

While Joseph and Madame Bridau were journeying from Orleans to Issoudun,
the Knights of Idleness perpetrated one of their best tricks. An old
Spaniard, a former prisoner of war, who after the peace had remained in
the neighborhood and did a small business in grain, came early one
morning to market, leaving his empty cart at the foot of the tower of
Issoudun. Maxence, who arrived at a rendezvous of the Knights, appointed
on that occasion at the foot of the tower, was soon assailed with the
whispered question, “What are we to do to-night?”

“Here’s Pere Fario’s cart,” he answered. “I nearly cracked my shins over
it. Let us get it up on the embankment of the tower in the first place,
and we’ll make up our minds afterwards.”

When Richard Coeur-de-Lion built the tower of Issoudun he raised it, as
we have said, on the ruins of the basilica, which itself stood above the
Roman temple and the Celtic Dun. These ruins, each representing several
centuries, form a mound full of the monuments of three distinct ages.
The tower is, therefore, the apex of a cone, from which the descent is
steep on all sides, and which is only approached by a series of steps.
To give an idea of the height of this tower, we may compare it to the
obelisk of Luxor on its pedestal. The pedestal of the tower of Issoudun,
which hid within its breast such archaeological treasures, was eighty
feet high on the side towards the town. In an hour the cart was taken
off its wheels and hoisted, piece by piece, to the top of the embankment
at the foot of the tower itself,--a work somewhat like that of soldiers
who carried artillery over the pass of the Grand Saint-Bernard. The
cart was then remounted on its wheels, and the Knights, by this time
hungry and thirsty, returned to Mere Cognette’s, where they were soon
seated round the table in the low room, laughing at the grimaces Fario
would make when he came after his barrow in the morning.

The Knights did not play such capers every night. The combined genius
of Sganarelle, Mascarille, and Scapin would not invent three hundred and
sixty-five pieces of mischief a year. Circumstances were not always
propitious: sometimes the moon shone clear, or the last prank had
irritated their betters; then one or another of their number refused to
share in some proposed outrage because a relation was involved. But if
the scamps were not at Mere Cognette’s every night, they always met
during the day, enjoying together hunting, the autumn vintages, and
winter skating. Among this assemblage of twenty youths, all of them at
war with the social somnolence of the place, some were more closely
allied to Max and made him their idol. A character like his often
fascinates other youths. The two grandsons of Madame Hochon--Francois
Hochon and Baruch Borniche--were his henchmen. These young fellows,
accepting the general opinion of the left-handed parentage of Lousteau,
looked upon Max as their cousin. Max, moreover, was liberal in lending
them money for their pleasures, which their grandfather Hochon refused;
he took them hunting, let them see life, and exercised more influence
over them than their own family. They were both orphans, and were kept,
although each had attained his majority, under the guardianship of
Monsieur Hochon, for reasons which will be explained when Monsieur
Hochon himself comes upon the scene.

At this particular moment Francois and Baruch (we will call them by
their Christian names for clearness) were sitting, one on each side of
Max, at the middle of a table lit by the fuliginous gleams of four
tallow candles. A dozen to fifteen bottles of various wines had just
been drunk, for only eleven of the Knights were present. Baruch--whose
name indicates that Calvinism still kept some hold on Issoudun--said
to Max, as the wine began to unloose tongues,--

“You are threatened in your stronghold.”

“What do you mean by that?” asked Max.

“Why, my grandmother has had a letter from Madame Bridau, who is her
goddaughter, saying that she and her son are coming here. My grandmother
has been getting two rooms ready for them.”

“What’s that to me?” said Max, taking up his glass and swallowing the
contents at a gulp with a comic gesture.

Max was then thirty-four years old. A candle near him threw a gleam
upon his soldierly face, lit up his brow, and brought out his clear
skin, ardent eyes, and black slightly curling hair that bristled from
the forehead in those five black tongues our ancestors called the “five
points.” Despite this sharp contrast of black and white, Max’s face was
very sweet, owing to an outline like Raphael’s Madonnas and a well-cut
mouth whose lips smiled graciously, giving a distinctive expression. The
rich coloring of a Berrichon cheek added to his kindly good-humor. When
he laughed heartily, he showed thirty-two teeth worthy of a pretty
woman’s mouth. About five feet six inches, the young man was well
proportioned—neither too stout nor too thin. His hands, carefully kept,
were white and rather handsome; but his feet recalled the suburb and the
foot-soldier of the Empire. Max would have made a good general of
division; he had shoulders worth a fortune to a marshal of France and a
breast broad enough to wear all the orders of Europe. Every movement
betrayed intelligence; born with grace and charm, like nearly all
children of love, the noble blood of his real father showed in him.

“Don’t you know, Max,” cried the son of a former surgeon-major named
Goddet--now the best doctor in the town--from the other end of the
table, “that Madame Hochon’s goddaughter is the sister of Rouget? If she
is coming here with her son, no doubt she means to make sure of getting
the property when he dies, and then--good-by to your harvest!”

Max frowned. Then, with a look that ran from one face to another round
the table, he watched the effect of this announcement, and again
replied,--

“What’s that to me?”

“But,” said Francois, “I should think that if old Rouget revoked his
will,--in case he has made one in favor of the Rabouilleuse--”

Here Max cut short his henchman’s speech. “I’ve stopped the mouths of
people who have dared to meddle with you, my dear Francois,” he said;
“and this is the way you pay your debts? You use a contemptuous nickname
in speaking of a woman to whom I am known to be attached.”

Max had never before said as much about his relations with the person
to whom Francois had just applied a name under which she was known at
Issoudun. The late prisoner at Cabrera—the major of the grenadiers of
the Guard—knew enough of honor to judge rightly as to the causes of
society’s disesteem for him. He had therefore never allowed any one to
speak to him on the subject of Mademoiselle Flore Brazier, the
servant-mistress of Jean-Jacques Rouget, so energetically termed a
“slut” by the respectable Madame Hochon. Everybody knew it was too
ticklish a subject with Max to be broached unless he began it; and
hitherto he had never begun. To risk his anger was altogether too
dangerous; so even his best friends had never joked him about the
Rabouilleuse. When they talked of his liaison with the girl before
Major Potel and Captain Renard, with whom he lived on intimate terms,
Potel would reply,--

“If he is the natural brother of Jean-Jacques Rouget where else would
you have him live?”

“Besides, after all,” added Captain Renard, “the girl is a worthless
piece, and if Max does live with her where’s the harm?”

After this snub, Francois could not at once catch up his thread; but he
was less able to do so when Max said to him, gently,--

“Go on.”

“Faith, no!” cried Francois.

“You needn’t get angry, Max,” said young Goddet; “didn’t we agree to
talk freely to each other at Mere Cognette’s? Shouldn’t we all be mortal
enemies if we remembered outside what is said, or thought, or done
here? All the town calls Flore Brazier the Rabouilleuse; and if Francois
did happen to let the nickname slip out, is that a crime against the
Order of Idleness?”

“No,” said Max, “but against our personal friendship. However, I
thought better of it; I recollected we were in session, and that was
why I said, ‘Go on.’”

A deep silence followed. The pause became so embarrassing that Max
broke it by exclaiming:--

“I’ll go on for him,” (sensation) “--for all of you,” (amazement) “--
and tell you what you are thinking” (profound sensation). “You think
that Flore, the Rabouilleuse, La Brazier, the housekeeper of Pere
Rouget,--for they call him so, that old bachelor, who can never have any
children!--you think, I say, that that woman supplies all my wants ever
since I came back to Issoudun. If I am able to throw three hundred
francs a month to the dogs, and treat you to suppers,--as I do to-night,
--and lend money to all of you, you think I get the gold out of
Mademoiselle Flore Brazier’s purse? Well, yes” (profound sensation).
“Yes, ten thousand times yes! Yes, Mademoiselle Brazier is aiming
straight for the old man’s property.”

“She gets it from father to son,” observed Goddet, in his corner.

“You think,” continued Max, smiling at Goddet’s speech, “that I intend
to marry Flore when Pere Rouget dies, and so this sister and her son,
of whom I hear to-night for the first time, will endanger my future?”

“That’s just it,” cried Francois.

“That is what every one thinks who is sitting round this table,” said
Baruch.

“Well, don’t be uneasy, friends,” answered Max. “Forewarned is
forearmed! Now then, I address the Knights of Idleness. If, to get rid
of these Parisians I need the help of the Order, will you lend me a
hand? Oh! within the limits we have marked out for our fooleries,” he
added hastily, perceiving a general hesitation. “Do you suppose I want
to kill them,--poison them? Thank God I’m not an idiot. Besides, if the
Bridaus succeed, and Flore has nothing but what she stands in, I should
be satisfied; do you understand that? I love her enough to prefer her to
Mademoiselle Fichet,--if Mademoiselle Fichet would have me.”

Mademoiselle Fichet was the richest heiress in Issoudun, and the hand
of the daughter counted for much in the reported passion of the younger
Goddet for the mother. Frankness of speech is a pearl of such price
that all the Knights rose to their feet as one man.

“You are a fine fellow, Max!”

“Well said, Max; we’ll stand by you!”

“A fig for the Bridaus!”

“We’ll bridle them!”

“After all, it is only three swains to a shepherdess.”

“The deuce! Pere Lousteau loved Madame Rouget; isn’t it better to love a
housekeeper who is not yoked?”

“If the defunct Rouget was Max’s father, the affair is in the family.”

“Liberty of opinion now-a-days!”

“Hurrah for Max!”

“Down with all hypocrites!”

“Here’s a health to the beautiful Flore!”

Such were the eleven responses, acclamations, and toasts shouted forth
by the Knights of Idleness, characteristic of their relaxed morality.
It is now easy to see what interest Max had in becoming their grand
master. By leading the young men of the best families in their follies
and amusements, and by doing them services, he meant to create a
support for himself when the day for recovering his position came. He
rose gracefully and waved his glass of claret, while all the others
waited eagerly for the coming allocution.

“As a mark of the ill-will I bear you, I wish you all a mistress who is
equal to the beautiful Flore! As to this irruption of relations, I
don’t feel any present uneasiness; and as to the future, we’ll see what
comes--”

“Don’t let us forget Fario’s cart!”

“Hang it! that’s safe enough!” said Goddet.

“Oh! I’ll engage to settle that business,” cried Max. “Be in the
market-place early, all of you, and let me know when the old fellow goes
for his cart.”

It was striking half-past three in the morning as the Knights slipped
out in silence to go to their homes; gliding close to the walls of the
houses without making the least noise, shod as they were in list shoes.
Max slowly returned to the place Saint-Jean, situated in the upper part
of the town, between the port Saint-Jean and the port Vilatte, the
quarter of the rich bourgeoisie. Maxence Gilet had concealed his fears,
but the news had struck home. His experience on the hulks at Cabrera
had taught him a dissimulation as deep and thorough as his corruption.
First, and above all, the forty thousand francs a year from landed
property which old Rouget owned was, let it be clearly understood, the
constituent element of Max’s passion for Flore Brazier. By his present
bearing it is easy to see how much confidence the woman had given him
in the financial future she expected to obtain through the infatuation
of the old bachelor. Nevertheless, the news of the arrival of the
legitimate heirs was of a nature to shake Max’s faith in Flore’s
influence. Rouget’s savings, accumulating during the last seventeen
years, still stood in his own name; and even if the will, which Flore
declared had long been made in her favor, were revoked, these savings
at least might be secured by putting them in the name of Mademoiselle
Brazier.

“That fool of a girl never told me, in all these seven years, a word
about the sister and nephews!” cried Max, turning from the rue de la
Marmouse into the rue l’Avenier. “Seven hundred and fifty thousand
francs placed with different notaries at Bourges, and Vierzon, and
Chateauroux, can’t be turned into money and put into the Funds in a
week, without everybody knowing it in this gossiping place! The most
important thing is to get rid of these relations; as soon as they are
driven away we ought to make haste to secure the property. I must think
it over.”

Max was tired. By the help of a pass-key, he let himself into Pere
Rouget’s house, and went to bed without making any noise, saying to
himself,--

“To-morrow, my thoughts will be clear.”

It is now necessary to relate where the sultana of the place Saint-Jean
picked up the nickname of “Rabouilleuse,” and how she came to be the
quasi-mistress of Jean-Jacques Rouget’s home.

As old Doctor Rouget, the father of Jean-Jacques and Madame Bridau,
advanced in years, he began to perceive the nonentity of his son; he
then treated him harshly, trying to break him into a routine that might
serve in place of intelligence. He thus, though unconsciously,
prepared him to submit to the yoke of the first tyranny that threw its
halter over his head.

Coming home one day from his professional round, the malignant and
vicious old man came across a bewitching little girl at the edge of some
fields that lay along the avenue de Tivoli. Hearing the horse, the child
sprang up from the bottom of one of the many brooks visible from the
heights of Issoudun, threading the meadows like ribbons of silver on a
green robe. Naiad-like, she rose suddenly on the doctor’s vision,
showing the loveliest virgin head that painters ever dreamed of. Old
Rouget, who knew the whole country-side, did not know this miracle of
beauty. The child, half naked, wore a forlorn petticoat of coarse
woollen stuff, woven in alternate strips of brown and white, full of
holes and very ragged. A sheet of rough writing paper, tied on by a
shred of osier, served her for a hat. Beneath this paper--covered with
pot-hooks and round O’s, from which it derived the name of “schoolpaper”
--the loveliest mass of blonde hair was twisted up and held by a comb
made to comb out the tails of horses. Her pretty tanned bosom and neck,
scarcely covered by a ragged fichu that was once a Madres handkerchief,
showed edges of white skin below the exposed sun-burned parts. One end
of her petticoat was drawn between the legs and fastened with a huge pin
in front, giving that garment the look of bathing drawers. The feet and
legs, visible through the clear water, attracted the eye by a delicacy
worthy of a medieval sculptor. The charming limbs exposed to the sun had
a ruddy tone not without beauty. The neck and bosom were worthy of
silks and cashmeres; and the nymph had blue eyes fringed with long
lashes, whose glance might have made a painter or a poet fall upon his
knees. The doctor, enough of an anatomist to trace the exquisite figure,
recognized the loss it would be to art if such lines were destroyed by
field labor.

“Where do you come from, little girl? I have never seen you before,”
said the old doctor, then sixty-two years of age. This scene took place
in the month of September, 1799.

“I belong in Vatan,” she answered.

Hearing Rouget’s voice, an ill-looking man, standing at some distance in
the deeper waters of the brook, raised his head. “What are you about,
Flore?” he said, “While you are talking instead of catching, the
creatures will get away.”

“Why have you come here from Vatan?” continued the doctor, paying no
heed to the interruption.

“I am catching crabs for my uncle Brazier here.”

“Rabouiller” is a Berrichon word which admirably describes the action
it expresses; namely, troubling the water of a brook, making it boil
and bubble with a branch whose end-shoots spread out like a racket. The
crabs, frightened by this operation, come hastily to the surface, and
in their flurry rush into the net the fisher has laid at a little
distance. Flore Brazier held her “rabouilloir” in her hand with the
natural grace of childlike innocence.

“Has your uncle got permission to hunt crabs?”

“Hey! are not we all under a Republic that is one and indivisible?”
cried the uncle from his station.

“We are under a Directory,” said the doctor, “and I know of no law which
allows a man to come from Vatan and fish in the territory of Issoudun”;
then he said to Flore, “Have you got a mother, little one!”

“No, monsieur; and my father is in the asylum at Bourges. He went mad
from a sun-stroke he got in the fields.”

“How much do you earn?”

“Five sous a day while the season lasts; I catch ‘em as far as the
Braisne. In harvest time, I glean; in winter, I spin.”

“You are about twelve years old?”

“Yes, monsieur.”

“Do you want to come with me? You shall be well fed and well dressed,
and have some pretty shoes.”

“No, my niece will stay with me; I am responsible to God and man for
her,” said Uncle Brazier who had come up to them. “I am her guardian,
d’ye see?”

The doctor kept his countenance and checked a smile which might have
escaped most people at the aspect of the man. The guardian wore a
peasant’s hat, rotted by sun and rain, eaten like cabbage leaves that
harbored caterpillars, and mended here and there with white thread.
Beneath the hat was a dark and sunken face, in which mouth, nose, and
eyes seemed four black spots. His forlorn jacket was patchwork, and his
trousers were of crash towelling.

“I am Doctor Rouget,” said that individual; “and as you are the guardian
of the child, bring her to my house, in the place Saint-Jean. It will
not be a bad day’s work for you; nor for her, either.”

Without waiting for an answer, and sure Uncle Brazier would soon bring
his pretty “rabouilleuse,” Doctor Rouget set spurs to his horse and
returned to Issoudun. He had hardly sat down to dinner before his cook
announced the arrival of the citoyen and citoyenne Brazier.

“Sit down,” said the doctor to the uncle and niece.

Flore and her guardian, still barefooted, looked round the doctor’s
dining-room with wondering eyes; never having seen its like before.

The house, which Rouget inherited from the Descoings estate, stands in
the middle of the place Saint-Jean, a long narrow square planted with a
few sickly lindens. The houses in this part of town are better built
than elsewhere, and that of the Descoings was one of the finest. It
stands opposite Monsieur Hochon’s house, and has three windows in front
on the first storey, and a porte-cochere on the ground-floor leading to
a courtyard and garden. Under the archway of the porte-cochere is the
door of a large hall lighted by two windows on the street. The kitchen
is behind this hall, part of the space used for a staircase to the
upper floor and attic. Beyond the kitchen is a wood-shed and wash-house,
a stable for two horses and a coach-house, over which are little lofts
for oats, hay, and straw, where the doctor’s servant slept.

The hall which the little peasant and her uncle admired is decorated
with wooden carvings of the time of Louis XV., painted gray, and a
handsome marble chimney-piece, over which Flore beheld herself in a
large mirror with a carved and gilded frame. On the panelled walls
hung several pictures, spoil of various religious houses, such as the
abbeys of Deols, Issoudun, Saint-Gildas, La Pree, Chezal-Beniot,
Saint-Sulpice, and the convents of Bourges and Issoudun, which kings
had enriched with Renaissance works. Among the pictures obtained by the
Descoings and inherited by Rouget were an Albano Holy Family, a Saint
Jerome of Domenichino, a Head of Christ by Gian Bellini, a Virgin of
Leonardo, a Bearing of the Cross by Titian, a Lazarus of Paul Veronese,
a Marriage of the Virgin by the priest Genois, two church paintings by
Rubens, and a replica of a picture by Perugino, perhaps by Perugino or
Raphael; and finally, two Correggios and one Andrea del Sarto.

The Descoings had culled these treasures from three hundred church
pictures, without knowing their value, selecting them for preservation.
Many were in magnificent frames, some still under glass. Perhaps it
was the beauty of the frames and the glass that led the Descoings to
retain the pictures. The furniture of the room was not wanting in the
sort of luxury prized in these days; at that time it had no value in
Issoudun. The clock on the mantle-shelf between two superb silver
candlesticks with six branches revealed the hand of Boulle. The armchairs
of carved oak, covered with tapestry due to the industry of high-born
women, would be treasured later; each was surmounted with a crown and
coat-of-arms. Between the windows stood a rich console, on whose marble
slab stood an immense China jar in which the doctor kept his tobacco.
But neither Rouget, nor his son, nor the cook took the slightest care of
these treasures. They spat upon a hearth of exquisite delicacy, whose
gilded mouldings were now green with verdigris. A handsome chandelier,
partly semi-transparent porcelain, and the ceiling were peppered with
black speckles, bearing witness to flies. The Descoings had draped the
windows with brocatelle curtains torn from some prior’s bed. To the left
of the entrance-door stood a chest or coffer worth many thousand francs,
which the doctor now used for a sideboard.

“Here, Fanchette,” cried Rouget to his cook, “bring two glasses; and
give us some of the old wine.”

Fanchette, a big Berrichon countrywoman, considered a better cook than
even La Cognette, ran in to receive the order with celerity that said
much for the doctor’s despotism and for her own curiosity.

“What is an acre of vineyard worth in your parts?” asked the doctor,
pouring out a glass of wine for Brazier.

“Three hundred francs in silver.”

“Well, then! leave your niece here as my servant; she shall have three
hundred francs in wages, and, as you are her guardian, you can take
them.”

“Every year?” exclaimed Brazier, with his eyes as wide as saucers.

“I leave that to your conscience,” said the doctor. “She is an orphan;
up to eighteen, she has no right to what she earns.”

“Twelve to eighteen--that’s six acres of vineyard!” said the uncle.
“Ay, she’s a pretty one, gentle as a lamb, well made and active, and
obedient as a kitten. She were the light o’ my poor brother’s eyes--”

“I will pay a year in advance,” observed the doctor.

“Bless me! say two years, and I’ll leave her with you, for she’ll be
better off with you than with us; my wife beats her, she can’t abide
her. There’s none but I to stand up for her, and the little saint of a
creature is as innocent as a new-born babe.”

When he heard the last part, the doctor, struck by the word “innocent,”
made a sign to the uncle and took him out into the courtyard and thence
to the garden; leaving the Rabouilleuse at the table with Fanchette and
Jean-Jacques, who immediately questioned her, and to whom she naively
related her meeting with the doctor.

“There now, my little darling, good-by,” said Uncle Brazier, coming back
and kissing Flore on the forehead; “you can well say I’ve made your
happiness by leaving you with this kind and worthy father of the poor;
you must obey him as you would me. Be a good girl, and behave nicely,
and do everything he tells you.”

“Get the room over mine ready,” said the doctor to Fanchette. “Little
Flore--I am sure she is worthy of the name--will sleep there in future.
To-morrow, we’ll send for a shoemaker and a dressmaker. Put another
plate on the table; she shall keep us company.”
