That evening, all Issoudun could talk of nothing else than the sudden
appearance of the little “rabouilleuse” in Doctor Rouget’s house. In
that region of satire the nickname stuck to Mademoiselle Brazier before,
during, and after the period of her good fortune.

The doctor no doubt intended to do with Flore Brazier, in a small way,
what Louis XV. did in a large one with Mademoiselle de Romans; but he
was too late about it; Louis XV. was still young, whereas the doctor
was in the flower of old age. From twelve to fourteen, the charming
little Rabouilleuse lived a life of unmixed happiness. Always well
dressed, and often better tricked out than the richest girls in
Issoudun, she sported a gold watch and jewels, given by the doctor to
encourage her studies, and she had a master who taught her to read,
write, and cipher. But the almost animal life of the true peasant had
instilled into Flore such deep repugnance to the bitter cup of
knowledge, that the doctor stopped her education at that point. His
intentions with regard to the child, whom he cleansed and clothed and
taught, were interpreted in a variety of ways by the cackling society of
the town, whose gossip often produced fatal blunders, like those
relating to the birth of Agathe and that of Max. It is not easy for a
country community to disentangle truth from conjecture and contradictory
reports to which a single fact gives rise. The provinces insist on full
explanations, and usually end by knowing everything. But each person
clings to the version he or she likes best; proclaims it, argues it,
and considers it the only true one. In spite of the strong light cast
on people’s lives by the constant spying of a little town, truth is
thus often obscured; to be recognized it needs the impartiality which
historians or superior minds acquire by a higher point of view.

“What do you suppose that old gorilla wants at his age with a little
girl only fifteen years old?” society was still saying two years after
the arrival of the Rabouilleuse.

“Ah! that’s true,” they answered, “his days of merry-making are long
past.”

“My dear fellow, the doctor is disgusted at the stupidity of his son,
and he persists in hating his daughter Agathe; it may be that he has
been living a decent life for the last two years, intending to marry
little Flore; suppose she were to give him a fine, active, strapping
boy, full of life like Max?” said one of the wise heads of the town.

“Bah! don’t talk nonsense! After such a life as Rouget and Lousteau led
from 1770 to 1787, is it likely that either of them would have children
at sixty-five years of age? The old villain has read the Scriptures, if
only as a doctor, and he is doing as David did in his old age; that’s
all.”

“They say that Brazier, when he is drunk, boasts in Vatan that he
cheated him,” cried one who always believed the worst of people.

“Good heavens! neighbor; what won’t they say at Issoudun?”

From 1800 to 1805, that is, for five years, the doctor enjoyed all the
pleasures of educating Flore without the annoyances the ambitions and
pretensions of Mademoiselle de Romans inflicted, it is said, on Louis
le Bien-Aime. The little Rabouilleuse was so satisfied when she compared
the life she led at the doctor’s with that she would have led at her
uncle Brazier’s, that she yielded no doubt to the exactions of her
master as if she had been an Eastern slave. With due deference to
makers of idylls and to philanthropists, the inhabitants of the
provinces have very little idea of certain virtues; their scruples are
roused by self-interest, not by any sentiment of the right or the
becoming. Raised from infancy with no prospect but poverty and ceaseless
labor, they consider anything that saves them from the hell of hunger
and toil as permissible, particularly if it is not contrary to law.
Exceptions are rare. Virtue, socially speaking, is the companion of a
comfortable life, and comes only with education.

Thus the Rabouilleuse was an object of envy to all the young
peasant-girls within ten miles, although her conduct, from a religious
point of view, was supremely reprehensible. Flore, born in 1787, grew up
in the midst of the saturnalias of 1793 and 1798, whose lurid gleams
penetrated these country regions, then deprived of priests and faith and
altars and religious ceremonies; where marriage was nothing more than
legal coupling, and revolutionary maxims left a deep impression. This
was markedly the case at Issoudun, a land where, as we have seen, revolt
of all kinds is traditional. In 1802, Catholic worship was scarcely
re-established. The Emperor found it difficult to obtain priests. In
1806, many parishes all over France were still widowed; so slowly were
the clergy, decimated by the scaffold, gathered together again after
their violent dispersion.

In 1802, therefore, nothing was likely to reproach Flore Brazier,
unless it might be her conscience; and conscience was sure to be
weaker than self-interest in the ward of Uncle Brazier. If, as
everybody chose to suppose, the cynical doctor was compelled by his age
to respect a child of fifteen, the Rabouilleuse was none the less
considered very “wide awake,” a term much used in that region. Still,
some persons thought she could claim a certificate of innocence from
the cessation of the doctor’s cares and attentions in the last two
years of his life, during which time he showed her something more than
coldness.

Old Rouget had killed too many people not to know when his own end was
nigh; and his notary, finding him on his death-bed, draped as it were in
the mantle of encyclopaedic philosophy, pressed him to make a provision
in favor of the young girl, then seventeen years old.

“So I do,” he said, cynically; “my death sets her at liberty.”

This speech paints the nature of the old man. Covering his evil doings
with witty sayings, he obtained indulgence for them, in a land where wit
is always applauded,--especially when addressed to obvious self-interest.
In those words the notary read the concentrated hatred of a man whose
calculations had been balked by Nature herself, and who revenged himself
upon the innocent object of an impotent love. This opinion was
confirmed to some extent by the obstinate resolution of the doctor to
leave nothing to the Rabouilleuse, saying with a bitter smile, when the
notary again urged the subject upon him,--

“Her beauty will make her rich enough!”
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
CHAPTER IX

Jean-Jacques Rouget did not mourn his father, though Flore Brazier did. The old doctor had made his son extremely unhappy, especially since he came of age in 1791; but he had given the little peasant-girl the material pleasures ideal to country-folk. When Fanchette asked Flore, after the funeral, “Well, what is to become of you, now that monsieur is dead?” Jean-Jacques’s eyes lighted up, and for the first time his dull face grew animated, showing feeling and seeming to brighten under the rays of a thought.

“Leave the room,” he said to Fanchette, who was clearing the table.

At seventeen, Flore retained that delicacy of feature and form, that distinction of beauty which attracted the doctor, and which women of the world know how to preserve, though it fades among peasant-girls like the flowers of the field. Nevertheless, the embonpoint that handsome countrywomen develop when they no longer live a life of toil and sunshine was already noticeable. Her bust had developed; the plump white shoulders were modelled on rich lines harmonizing with those of the throat, showing a few folds of flesh. But the face’s outline was still faultless, and the chin delicate.

“Flore,” said Jean-Jacques, in a trembling voice, “you feel at home in this house?”

“Yes, Monsieur Jean.”

As the heir was about to make his declaration, his tongue stiffened at recollection of the dead man just put in his grave, and a doubt seized him as to what lengths his father’s benevolence might have gone. Flore, quite unable to suspect his simplicity of mind, looked at her future master and waited, expecting him to go on; but he finally left her without knowing what to think of such obstinate silence. Whatever teaching the Rabouilleuse may have received from the doctor, it was long before she understood Jean-Jacques’s character, whose history we now present briefly.

At his father’s death, Jacques, then thirty-seven, was as timid and submissive to paternal discipline as a child of twelve. That timidity ought to explain his childhood, youth, and after-life to those reluctant to admit such characters; proofs are common everywhere. There are two species of timidity — of the mind and of the nerves; physical and moral. The one is independent of the other. The body may fear while the mind is calm, or vice versa. When both are united, that man will be a cipher all his life; such double-sided timidity makes what we call “an imbecile.” Often fine suppressed qualities hide within that imbecile. This double infirmity is produced quite as much by perfection of soul and organs as by defects still unstudied.

Jean-Jacques’s timidity came from a torpor of his faculties, which a great teacher or surgeon would have roused. In him, as in cretins, the sense of love had inherited a strength lacking to his mental qualities, though he had enough mind for ordinary affairs. The violence of passion, stripped of the ideal in which most young men expend it, only increased his timidity. He had never courted any woman in Issoudun. No young girl or matron would make advances to a young man of mean stature, awkward and shame-faced; his vulgar face, flattened features and pallid skin, made him look old before his time and was rendered still more hideous by large, prominent light-green eyes. A woman’s presence stultified him; passion drove him one way, lack of ideas held him back the other. Paralyzed between opposing forces, he had not a word to say and feared being spoken to; desire petrified his speech. Thus Jean-Jacques was solitary and sought solitude because there alone he was at his ease.

The doctor had seen, too late, the havoc wrought in his son by this temperament. He would have been glad to get him married; but to do that he must deliver him to an influence certain to become tyrannical, and the doctor hesitated. Was it not practically giving management of the property to a stranger? He knew how difficult it was to gain true indications of a woman’s moral character from studying a young girl. So, while searching for a daughter-in-law whose sentiments and education offered guarantees, he endeavored to push his son into avarice, meaning to give the poor fool an instinct that might take the place of intelligence.

He trained him in mechanical habits of life and instilled fixed ideas about investing revenues; he spared him chief difficulties of managing a fortune by leaving estates in good order and long-leased. Nevertheless, one fact escaped notice: Timidity, secretive like dissimulation, hid Jean-Jacques’s passionate love for the Rabouilleuse. Nothing, of course, could be more natural. Flore was the only woman living in the bachelor’s presence, the only one he could see at his ease; he secretly contemplated her at all hours. To him she was the light of his paternal home; she gave him the only pleasures that brightened his youth. Far from being jealous of his father, he rejoiced in the education the old man gave Flore: would it not make her easy to win? Passion that can reflect gives even ninnies a species of intelligence. In the lowest human creature we find an animal instinct whose persistency resembles thought.

The next day Flore, reflecting on his silence, waited for a momentous communication; though he kept near her and glanced at her with passion, Jean-Jacques found nothing to say. At last, when dessert was on the table, he recommenced the night-before scene.

“You like your life here?” he said.

“Yes, Monsieur Jean.”

“Well, stay here then.”

“Thank you, Monsieur Jean.”

This strange situation lasted three weeks. One night Flore, waking, heard regular breathing outside her door and, frightened, discovered Jean-Jacques crouched like a dog on the landing.

“He loves me,” she thought; “but he will get the rheumatism if he keeps it up.”

The next day Flore looked at her master with a certain expression. This mute almost instinctive love had touched her; she no longer thought him so ugly, though his forehead was crowned with pimples, signs of vitiated blood.

“You don’t want to go back to the fields, do you?” said Jean-Jacques when they were alone.

“Why do you ask me that?” she said.

“To know--” replied Rouget, turning the color of a boiled lobster.

“Do you wish to send me back?” she asked.

“No, mademoiselle.”

“Well, what is it you want to know? You have some reason--”

“Yes, I want to know--”

“What?” said Flore.

“You won’t tell me?” exclaimed Rouget.

“Yes I will, on my honor--”

“Ah! that’s it,” returned Rouget, with a frightened air. “Are you an honest girl?”

“I’ll take my oath--”

“Are you, truly?”

“Don’t you hear me tell you so?”

“Come; are you the same as when your uncle brought you here barefooted?”

“A fine question, faith!” cried Flore, blushing.

The heir lowered his head and did not raise it. Flore, amazed at such an encouraging sign from a man overcome by a fear of that nature, left the room.

Three days later, at dessert-time, Flore spoke first.

“Have you anything against me?”

“No, mademoiselle,” he answered, “No--” (a pause) “On the contrary.”

“You seemed annoyed the other day to hear I was an honest girl.”

“No, I only wished to know--” (a pause) “But you would not tell me--”

“On my word!” she said, “I will tell you the whole truth.”

“The whole truth about--my father?” he asked in a strangled voice.

“Your father,” she said, looking full into his eye, “was a worthy man--he liked a joke--What of that?--there was nothing in it. But, poor dear man, it wasn’t the will that was wanting. The truth is, he had some spite against you, I don’t know what, and he meant--oh! he meant you harm. Sometimes he made me laugh; but there! what of that?”

“Well, Flore,” said the heir, taking her hand, “as my father was nothing to you--”

“What did you suppose he was to me?” she cried, as if offended by an unworthy suspicion.

“Well, but just listen--”

“He was my benefactor, that was all. Ah! he would have liked to make me his wife, but--”

“But,” said Rouget, taking the hand Flore had snatched away, “if he was nothing to you you can stay here with me, can’t you?”

“If you wish it,” she said, dropping her eyes.

“No, no! if you wish it, you!” exclaimed Rouget. “Yes, you shall be mistress here. All that is here shall be yours; you shall take care of my property, it is almost yours now--for I love you; I have always loved you since the day you came and stood there--there!--with bare feet.”

Flore made no answer. When silence became embarrassing, Jean-Jacques had recourse to a terrible argument.

“Come,” he said, with visible warmth, “wouldn’t it be better than returning to the fields?”

“As you will, Monsieur Jean,” she answered.

Nevertheless, Jean-Jacques got no further. Men of his nature want certainty; the effort to avow their love costs so much they feel unable to go on. Ten months after his father’s death Jean-Jacques changed completely; his leaden face cleared, his whole countenance breathed happiness. Flore exacted that he should take care of his person, and her vanity was gratified seeing him well-dressed; she always stood on the sill to watch him start for a walk. The whole town noticed the changes that made a new man of the bachelor.

“Have you heard the news?” people said.

“What is it?”

“Jean-Jacques inherits everything from his father, even the Rabouilleuse.”

“Don’t you suppose the old doctor was wicked enough to provide a ruler for his son?”

“Rouget has got a treasure, that’s certain,” said everybody.

“She’s sly! She is very handsome, and she will make him marry her.”

“What luck that girl has had!”

“The luck that only comes to pretty girls.”

“Ah, bah! do you believe that? look at my uncle Borniche-Herau. You’ve heard of Mademoiselle Ganivet? she was as ugly as seven capital sins, but she got three thousand francs a year out of him.”

“Yes, but that was in 1778.”

“Still, Rouget is making a mistake. His father left him a good forty thousand francs’ income, and he ought to marry Mademoiselle Herau.”

“The doctor tried to arrange it, but she would not consent; Jean-Jacques is so stupid--”

“Stupid! why women are very happy with that style of man.”

“Is your wife happy?”

Such tattle ran through Issoudun. If people began by laughing at this quasi-marriage, they ended by praising Flore for devoting herself to the poor fellow. We now see how Flore obtained management of the Rouget household — from father to son, as young Goddet had said. It is desirable to sketch that management for the edification of old bachelors.

Fanchette, the cook, was the only person in Issoudun who thought it wrong that Flore should be queen over Jean-Jacques and his home. She protested against the immorality of the connection, taking a tone of injured virtue; the fact being she was humiliated by having, at her age, a crab-girl for a mistress — a child brought barefoot into the house. Fanchette owned three hundred francs a year in the Funds, for the doctor made her invest her savings, and he had left her as much more in an annuity; she could therefore live without working, and she quit the house nine months after the funeral, April 15, 1806. That date may indicate when Flore ceased to be an honest girl.

The Rabouilleuse, clever enough to foresee Fanchette’s probable defection — there is nothing like power for teaching policy — was resolved to do without a servant. For six months she had studied, without seeming to, the culinary operations that made Fanchette a cordon-bleu. In choice living, doctors are on a par with bishops. The doctor had honed Fanchette’s talents to perfection. In the provinces, lack of occupation turns activity of mind towards the kitchen. People do not dine as luxuriously as in Paris, but they dine better; dishes are studied. We often find some Careme in petticoats, an unrecognized genius able to serve a simple dish of haricot-beans worthy of Rossini’s nod.

When studying for his degree in Paris, the doctor had followed chemistry under Rouelle, and gathered ideas he later used in the chemistry of cooking. His memory is famous in Issoudun for certain improvements little known outside Berry. He discovered that an omelette is more delicate when whites and yolks are not beaten violently together; whites should be beaten to a froth and yolks added gently by degrees; moreover a frying-pan should never be used, but a “cagnard” of porcelain or earthenware. The “cagnard” is a thick dish standing on four feet so air circulates under it. The doctor had also found a way to prevent the tartness of browned butter; his secret, kept to his own kitchen, has been lost.

Flore, a born fryer and roaster, soon surpassed Fanchette. In making herself a cordon-bleu she thought of Jean-Jacques’s comfort; though dainty, she spent activity upon household matters. She rubbed up the furniture till it shone, and kept everything clean. She managed avalanches of soiled linen and the “wash,” done thrice a year by provincial usage. She mended linen carefully. Desirous of learning the family property’s secret, she acquired Rouget’s limited business knowledge, increased it by conversations with the notary Monsieur Heron, and gave excellent advice to Jean-Jacques. Sure of being always mistress, she was eager about the old bachelor’s interests as if they were her own. She was not obliged to guard against her uncle’s exactions, for two months before the doctor’s death Brazier died of a fall leaving a wine-shop, where, since his rise in fortune, he spent most of his time. Flore had also lost her father; thus she served her master with all the affection an orphan thankful to make herself a home would feel.

This period was paradise to poor Jean-Jacques, who acquired gentle habits of an animal, trained into monastic regularity. He slept late. Flore, up at daybreak attending housekeeping, woke him so he would find breakfast ready. After breakfast, about eleven, Jean-Jacques went to walk; talked with people he met, and came home at three to read the papers — those of the department and a Paris journal received three days late, well greased by many hands. The old bachelor thus got through the day until dinner; over that meal he spent as much time as possible. Flore told him town news, repeating carefully picked-up cackle. Towards eight the lights were put out. Going to bed early is a saving of fire and candles common in the provinces, which contributes to empty-mindedness. Too much sleep dulls the brain.

Such was their life for nine years, the great events being a few journeys to Bourges, Vierzon, Chateauroux, or farther if notaries had investments. Rouget lent his money at five percent on a first mortgage, with release of the wife’s rights if married. He never lent more than a third of a property’s value, and required notes payable to his order for an additional interest of two and a half percent over the loan. Such were his father’s rules. Usury, that clog upon peasantry ambition, is the destroyer of country regions. This levy of seven and a half percent seemed reasonable to borrowers, and Rouget had his choice of investments; notaries, who got fine commissions, notified the old bachelor.

During these nine years Flore obtained absolute control over her master. From the first she treated him familiarly; then, surpassing him in superiority of mind and force of character, he became in fact the servant of his servant. Elderly child that he was, he met this mastery half-way by letting Flore take such care of him that she treated him as a mother treats a son; he ended by clinging to her as a child dependent on a mother’s protection. Other ties bound them as well. Flore kept the house and managed all business. Jean-Jacques left everything to her so completely that life without her would have seemed impossible. In every way she had become his one need; she indulged all his fancies, knowing them well. He loved to see her bright face always smiling — the only face that had ever smiled upon him. This purely material happiness, expressed in homely provincial words, was like a reflection of his inward content. When Flore’s brightness was clouded, Jean-Jacques’s state revealed her power over him, and to make sure of it she sometimes used it. Using power means, with women of her class, abusing it. The Rabouilleuse no doubt made him play scenes buried in private life’s mysteries, where the magnificent and horrible meet. Flore felt so secure of her power that, unfortunately for both, it did not occur to her to make him marry her.

Towards the close of 1815, Flore, then twenty-seven, had reached full beauty. Plump and fresh, white as a Norman countrywoman, she was the ideal of a “buxom housewife.” Her beauty, that of a handsome barmaid though higher in type, gave her a likeness to Mademoiselle George in her prime, setting aside the latter’s imperial dignity. Flore had dazzling white round arms, ample modelling, satiny skin, and alluring outlines. Her expression was one of sweetness and tenderness; her glance evoked a vulgar joy rather than commanding respect. In 1816 the Rabouilleuse saw Maxence Gilet and fell in love at first sight. Her heart was cleft by the mythological arrow. Flore was too handsome to be disdained, and Max accepted his conquest.

Thus at twenty-eight the Rabouilleuse felt for the first time a true, idolatrous love. As soon as the penniless officer learned Flore and Jean-Jacques’s situations, he saw more than an “amourette” in intimacy with the Rabouilleuse. He asked nothing better than to take up abode at the Rougets’, recognizing the feeble nature of the old bachelor. Flore’s passion affected household affairs. For a month the old man, now excessive in timidity, saw the laughing kindly face of his mistress change to something gloomy and sullen. She made him endure flashes of temper like a married man whose wife meditates infidelity. When, after a cruel rebuff, he asked the reason of the change, her eyes were so full of hatred and her voice so contemptuous that he quailed.

“Good heavens!” she cried; “you have neither heart nor soul! Sixteen years I have spent my youth in this house, and I have only just found out you have a stone there (striking her breast). For two months you have seen that brave captain, a victim of the Bourbons, cut out for a general and down in poverty, hunted into a hole where there’s no way to make a penny! He’s forced to sit on a stool all day in the mayor’s office to earn—what? Six hundred miserable francs,—and here are you, with six hundred and fifty-nine thousand well invested, and sixty thousand francs’ income,—thanks to me, who never spend more than three thousand a year, everything included, even my own clothes,—and you never think of offering him a home here, though the second floor is empty! You’d rather rats and mice run riot in it than put a human being there,—and he a lad your father always allowed to be his own son! Do you want to know what you are? A fratricide! And I know why. You see I take an interest in him, and that provokes you. Stupid as you seem, you have more spite than the spitefullest of men. Well, yes! I do take an interest in him, and a keen one—”

“But, Flore—”

“‘But, Flore’ indeed! You may go and find another Flore (if you can!), for I hope this glass of wine may poison me if I don’t get away from your dungeon of a house. I haven’t, God be thanked, cost you one penny during twelve years I’ve been with you, and you have had my company into the bargain. I could have earned my living anywhere with the work I’ve done—washing, ironing, looking after linen, going to market, cooking, taking care of your interests before everything, slaving from morning till night—and this is my reward!”

“But, Flore—”

“Oh, yes, ‘Flore’! find another Flore, if you can, at your time of life, fifty-one and getting feeble,—for the way your health is failing is frightful, I know that! and besides, you are none too amusing—”

“But, Flore—”

“Let me alone!”

She went out, slamming the door with a violence that echoed through the house. Jean-Jacques softly opened and went, more softly, into the kitchen where she muttered.

“But, Flore,” said the poor sheep, “this is the first time I have heard of this wish of yours; how do you know whether I will agree?”

“In the first place,” she said, “there ought to be a man in the house. Everybody knows you have ten, fifteen, twenty thousand here; if they came to rob you we should both be murdered. For my part, I don’t care to wake up some fine morning chopped in quarters, as happened to that poor servant-girl who defended her master. Well! if robbers knew there was a man in the house as brave as Caesar—Max could swallow three burglars as quick as a flash—then I should sleep easy. People may talk—that I love him, that I adore him—and some say this and some that! Do you know what you ought to say? You ought to answer that your father told you on his deathbed to take care of his poor Max. That will stop tongues; every stone in Issoudun can tell you he paid Max’s schooling—and so! Here’s nine years I have eaten your bread—”

“Flore,—Flore!”

“—and many in this town have paid court to me! Gold chains here, watches there—what don’t they offer me? ‘My little Flore,’ they say, ‘why won’t you leave that old fool of a Rouget?’ ‘I leave him!’ I always answer, ‘a poor innocent like that? what would become of him? No, where the kid is tethered, let her browse—’”

“Yes, Flore; I’ve none but you in this world, and you make me happy. If it will give you pleasure, my dear, well, we will have Maxence Gilet here; he can eat with us—”

“Heavens! I should hope so!”

“There, there! don’t get angry—”

“Enough for one is enough for two,” she answered laughing. “I’ll tell you what you can do, my lamb. Go and walk near the Mayor’s office at four, and manage to meet Monsieur Gilet and invite him to dinner. If he makes excuses, tell him it will give me pleasure; he is too polite to refuse. After dinner, at dessert, if he tells you about his misfortunes, and the hulks and so forth—for you can easily get him to talk—then you can offer him to come and live here. If he objects, never mind; I shall know how to settle it.”

Walking slowly along boulevard Baron, the old celibate reflected, as much as he had mind to reflect, over this incident. If he were to part from Flore (the mere thought confused him) where could he find another woman? Should he marry? At his age he should be married for his money, and a legitimate wife would use him far more cruelly than Flore. Besides, being deprived of her tenderness, even if mere pretence, caused him horrible anguish. He was therefore as polite to Captain Gilet as he knew how. The invitation was given, as Flore had requested, before witnesses, to guard the hero’s honor.

A reconciliation took place; but from that day Jean-Jacques noticed many trifles that betokened a total change in his mistress’s affections. For two or three weeks Flore complained to tradespeople and women about Monsieur Rouget’s tyranny in inviting his natural brother to live with him. No one was taken in by the comedy; Flore was looked upon as a wonderfully clever, artful creature. Old Rouget really found himself comfortable after Max became master of his house; he gained a companion who paid him attentions without showing servility. Gilet talked, discussed politics, and sometimes walked with Rouget. After Max was installed, Flore did not choose to do cooking; she said it spoiled her hands. At the request of the grand master of the Order of the Knights of Idleness, Mere Cognette produced a relative, an old maid whose master, a curate, had lately died without leaving her anything,—an excellent cook who declared she would devote herself for life or death to Max and Flore. In the name of the two powers, Mere Cognette promised her an annuity of three hundred francs a year at the end of ten years if she served loyally and discreetly. The Vedie, as she was called, had a face pitted by smallpox and was ugly.

After the new cook entered upon her duties, the Rabouilleuse took the title Madame Brazier. She wore corsets; she had silk, or handsome woollen and cotton dresses, expensive neckerchiefs, embroidered caps and collars, lace ruffles at her throat, boots instead of shoes, and adopted richness and elegance renewing her youthfulness. She was like a rough diamond needing cutting to bring out full value. Her desire was to do honor to Max. At the end of the first year, in 1817, she brought a horse, called English, from Bourges for the poor cavalry captain, weary of walking. Max had picked up in Issoudun an old lancer of the Imperial Guard, a Pole named Kouski, now very poor, who asked nothing better than to quarter himself in Rouget’s house as the captain’s servant. Max idolized Kouski, especially after the duel with three royalists. So, from 1817, the household was made up of five persons, three of whom were masters, and expenses advanced to about eight thousand francs a year.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
CHAPTER X

At the time Madame Bridau returned to Issoudun to save—Maitre Desroches said—an inheritance in danger, Jean-Jacques Rouget had gradually become semi-vegetative. After Max’s instalment, Flore set the table on an episcopal footing. Rouget, exposed to good living, ate more and more, tempted by the Vedie’s excellent dishes. He did not grow fatter; he weakened daily, fatigued perhaps by digestion, and his eyes had dark circles. When neighbors questioned him, he always said he was never better. Thought mentally deficient, people did not notice his declining faculties. His love for Flore kept him alive; he existed only for her, obeying her slightest look, watching her like a dog. As Madame Hochon remarked, at fifty-seven he seemed older than Monsieur Hochon, an octogenarian.

Everyone supposed, rightly, that Max’s appartement matched his charm. In six years our captain had improved the comfort and adorned it for himself and Flore. But it was only Issoudun luxury—colored tiles, somewhat elegant wallpapers, mahogany furniture, gilt mirrors, muslin curtains with red borders, a canopy bed, provincial draperies—luxury in Issoudun though common in Paris. One unusual thing caused talk: a rush-matting on the stairs, doubtless to muffle footsteps. Max came in at daybreak without waking anyone; Rouget did not suspect his guest was an accomplice in the Knights of Idleness’ nocturnal performances.

About eight the next morning Flore, in a cotton dressing-gown with narrow pink stripes, a lace cap, and furred slippers, softly opened Max’s door; seeing him asleep she stood beside the bed.

“He came in so late!” she thought. “Half-past three. He must have a good constitution. I wonder what they did last night.”

“Oh, there you are, my little Flore!” said Max, waking like a soldier trained to be ready instantly.

“You are sleepy; I’ll go away.”

“No, stay; there’s something serious going on.”

“Were you up to mischief last night?”

“Ah, bah! It concerns you and me and that old fool. You never told me he had a family! Well, his family are coming—coming here—no doubt to turn us out.”

“Ah! I’ll shake him well,” said Flore.

“Mademoiselle Brazier,” said Max gravely, “things are too serious for giddiness. Send me my coffee; I’ll take it in bed and think over what we had better do. Come back at nine and we’ll talk. Meanwhile, act as if you’ve heard nothing.”

Frightened, Flore went to make his coffee; a quarter-hour later Baruch burst into Max’s room, crying to the grand master,—

“Fario is hunting for his barrow!”

In five minutes Max was dressed and in the street; though he sauntered, he soon reached the tower embankment, where a crowd had gathered.

“What is it?” asked Max, pushing through and reaching the Spaniard.

Fario was a withered little man, ugly as though blue-blooded grandee. His fiery, close-set eyes were piercing; calm, slow in movement, people called him “goodman Fario.” His gingerbread skin and gentle manner hid a half-Moorish peasant nature from Granada, long unroused from phlegmatic indolence.

“Are you sure,” Max asked after hearing his grievance, “that you left your cart here? There are no thieves in Issoudun.”

“I left it just there—”

“If the horse was harnessed, hasn’t he drawn it somewhere?”

“Here’s the horse,” said Fario, pointing to the animal harnessed thirty feet away.

Max gravely went to where the horse stood, from which one could see the bottom of the tower at the top of the embankment; the crowd followed, as the scoundrel wanted.

“Has anybody thoughtlessly put a cart in his pocket?” cried Francois.

“Turn out your pockets, all of you!” said Baruch.

Laughter rose. Fario swore; with a Spaniard oaths mark high anger.

“Was your cart light?” asked Max.

“Light!” cried Fario. “If those who laugh had it on their feet, their corns would never hurt them again.”

“Well, it must be devilishly light,” answered Max, “for look there!” pointing to the foot of the tower; “it has flown up the embankment.”

All eyes lifted, and for a moment there was uproar in the market-place. Each pointed at the bewitched barrow and talked.

“The devil makes common cause with the inn-keepers,” said Goddet to the astonished Spaniard. “He means to teach you not to leave your cart about in the streets, but to put it in the tavern stables.”

The crowd hooted; Fario was thought a miser.

“Come, my good fellow,” said Max, “don’t lose heart. We’ll go up and see how your barrow got there. Thunder and cannon! we’ll lend you a hand! Come along, Baruch.”

“As for you,” he whispered to Francois, “get the people to stand back, and make sure there is nobody at the foot of the embankment when you see us at the top.”

Fario, Max, Baruch, and three other knights climbed. During the perilous ascent Max and Fario noticed no trace of the barrow’s passage; Fario imagined witchcraft and lost his head. At the top it seemed impossible the cart had got there.

“How shall I ever get it down?” said the Spaniard, whose small eyes for the first time showed fear; his yellow face whitened.

“How?” said Max. “Why, that’s not difficult.”

Taking advantage of Fario’s stupefaction, he raised the barrow by the shafts with his robust arms and prepared to fling it down, calling as it left his grasp, “Look out there, below!”

No accident happened; the crowd, persuaded by Francois and curious, had retired to a distance. The cart dashed to pieces in a very picturesque manner.

“There! you have got it down,” said Baruch.

“Ah, brigands! ah, scoundrels!” cried Fario; “perhaps it was you who brought it up here!”

Max, Baruch, and their comrades laughed at the Spaniard’s rage.

“I wanted to do you a service,” said Max coolly, “and in handling the damned thing I came very near flinging myself after it; and this is how you thank me, is it? What country do you come from?”

“I come from a country where they never forgive,” replied Fario, trembling. “My cart will be the cab in which you shall drive to the devil!—unless,” he suddenly grew meek, “you give me a new one.”

“We will talk about that,” said Max, descending.

At the bottom Max took Fario by the jacket and said,—

“Yes, my good Fario, I’ll give you a magnificent cart, if you will give me two hundred and fifty francs; but I won’t warrant it to go, like this one, up a tower.”

At this jest Fario cooled as though making a bargain.

“Damn it!” he said, “give me the wherewithal to replace my barrow, and it will be the best use you ever made of old Rouget’s money.”

Max turned livid; he raised his fist to strike, but Baruch, knowing the blow would be misdirected, plucked Fario away and whispered,—

“Don’t commit such a folly!”

Called to order, the grand master laughed and said to Fario,—

“If I, by accident, broke your barrow, and you try to slander me, we are quits.”

“Not yet,” muttered Fario. “But I am glad to know what my barrow was worth.”

“Ah, Max, you’ve found your match!” said a spectator not of the Order.

“Adieu, Monsieur Gilet. I haven’t thanked you yet for lending me a hand,” cried the Spaniard, as he kicked his horse and vanished amid hurrahs.

“We will keep the tires of the wheels for you,” shouted a wheelwright who had come to inspect the damage.

One shaft stuck upright in the ground as straight as a tree. Max stood by, pale and thoughtful, annoyed by Fario’s speech. For five days the Spaniard’s barrow was all Issoudun talked of; Goddet said it would travel abroad—indeed the tale reached Berry, and in a week the affair, to Fario’s satisfaction, was the talk of three departments. Because of the Spaniard’s vindictive speech, Max and the Rabouilleuse were whispered about in Issoudun and openly discussed in Bourges, Vatan, Vierzon, and Chateauroux. Maxence Gilet knew how envenomed such comments would become.

“We can’t stop their tongues,” he said. “Ah! I did a foolish thing!”

“Max!” said Francois, taking his arm. “They are coming to-night.”

“They! Who!”

“The Bridaus. My grandmother has had a letter from her goddaughter.”

“Listen, my boy,” said Max low. “I have been thinking deeply. Neither Flore nor I ought to seem opposed to the Bridaus. If these heirs are to be got rid of, it is for you Hochons to drive them out. Find out what sort of people they are. Tomorrow at Mere Cognette’s, after I’ve taken their measure, we can decide what to do, and how to set your grandfather against them.”

“The Spaniard found the flaw in Max’s armor,” said Baruch to his cousin Francois as they turned into Monsieur Hochon’s and watched their comrade entering his own door.

While Max was thus occupied, Flore, despite advice, could not restrain her wrath and burst upon the poor bachelor. When Jean-Jacques incurred her anger, his little attentions and vulgar fondlings—his joy—were suppressed. Flore sent him, as children say, into disgrace. No more tender glances or caressing words—“my kitten,” “my old darling,” “my bibi,” “my rat”—were heard. A cold, sharp, ironic “you” cut through the heart of the miserable old bachelor; the “you” was a declaration of war. Instead of helping him with his toilet, handing what he wanted, forestalling his wishes, or admiring him—“You look as fresh as a rose!”—she left him to dress alone. If he called, she answered from the foot of the stairs, “I can’t do everything at once; how can I look after your breakfast and wait upon you? Are not you big enough to dress yourself?”

“Oh, dear! what have I done to displease her?” he asked that morning, after a rebuff when calling for shaving-water.

“Vedie, take up the hot water,” cried Flore.

“Vedie!” exclaimed the poor man, stupefied. “Vedie, what is the matter with Madame this morning?”

Flore Brazier required her master, Vedie, Kouski, and Max to call her Madame.

“She seems to have heard something about you which isn’t to your credit,” Vedie said, pretending great concern. “I’m only a poor servant and have no right to poke my nose; but I say you may search through all the women and you won’t find the equal of Madame. You ought to kiss the ground she steps on. Goodness! if you make her unhappy, you’ll only spoil your own life. There she is, poor thing, with her eyes full of tears.”

Vedie left the poor man cast down; he dropped into an armchair and stared into vacancy like the melancholy imbecile he was, and forgot to shave. These alternations of tenderness and severity had on this feeble creature, whose only life was through his amorous fibre, the same morbid effect that sudden climatic changes have on the body. It was a moral pleurisy wearing him out like disease. Flore alone could thus affect him; to her and her alone he was as good as foolish.

“Well, haven’t you shaved yet?” she said, appearing at his door.

Her sudden presence made him start; from pale and cast down he grew red, yet dared not complain.

“Your breakfast is waiting,” she added. “You can come down as you are, in dressing-gown and slippers; for you’ll breakfast alone, I can tell you.”

Without waiting, she disappeared. To breakfast alone was his worst punishment; he loved to talk to her as he ate. At the foot of the stairs he was seized with a coughing fit; emotion excited his catarrh.

“Cough away!” said Flore in the kitchen, without caring if he heard. “Confound the old wretch! he is able enough to get over it without bothering others. If he coughs up his soul, it will only be after—”

Such were the amenities the Rabouilleuse addressed to Rouget when angry. The poor man sat in deep distress at a corner of the table and looked at his old furniture and pictures with a disconsolate air.

“You might at least have put on a cravat,” said Flore. “Do you think it pleasant for people to see such a neck as yours, redder and more wrinkled than a turkey’s?”

“But what have I done?” he asked, lifting his big light-green eyes, full of tears, to her and trying to meet her hard countenance.

“What have you done?” she exclaimed. “As if you didn’t know! Your sister Agathe—who is as much your sister as I am sister of the tower of Issoudun, if one’s to believe your father, and who has no claim at all upon you—is coming from Paris with her son, a miserable two-penny painter, to see you.”

“My sister and my nephews coming to Issoudun!” he said, bewildered.

“Oh, yes! play surprised; try to make me believe you didn’t send for them! Don’t fash yourself; we won’t trouble your Parisians—before they set their feet in this house we shall have shaken the dust off ours. Max and I will be gone, never to return. As for your will, I’ll tear it in quarters under your nose and to your very beard—do you hear? Leave your property to your family if you don’t think we are your family; and then see if you’ll be loved for yourself by a lot of people who have not seen you for thirty years—who in fact have never seen you! Is that the sort of sister who can take my place? A pinchbeck saint!”

“If that’s all, my little Flore,” said the old man, “I won’t receive my sister, or my nephews. I swear this is the first I heard of their coming. It is all got up by Madame Hochon—a sanctimonious old—”

Max, who had overheard, entered suddenly and said in a masterful tone,—

“What’s all this?”

“My good Max,” said the old man, glad for the soldier’s protection who by agreement with Flore always took his side, “I swear by all that is most sacred, I now hear this news for the first time. I never wrote to my sister; my father made me promise not to leave her any property; to leave it to the Church sooner than to her. Well, I won’t receive my sister Agathe or her sons—”

“Your father was wrong, my dear Jean-Jacques, and Madame Brazier is still more wrong,” answered Max. “Your father no doubt had his reasons, but he is dead, and his hatred should die with him. Your sister is your sister, and your nephews your nephews. You owe it to yourself to welcome them, and you owe it to us as well. What would people say in Issoudun? Thunder! I’ve enough on my shoulders, without hearing people say we shut you up and don’t allow you a will of your own, or that we influence you against your relations and are trying to get hold of your property. The devil take me if I don’t pull up stakes if calumny is flung at me! the other is bad enough! Let’s eat our breakfast.”

Flore, now mild as a weasel, helped Vedie set the table. Old Rouget, full of admiration for Max, took him by both hands and led him to a window recess, saying low,—

“Ah! Max, if I had a son, I couldn’t love him better than I love you. Flore is right: you two are my real family. You are a man of honor, Max, and what you have just said is true.”

“You ought to receive and entertain your sister and her son, but not change your arrangements about property,” Max said. “In that way you will do right in the world’s eyes and yet keep your promise to your father.”

“Well! my dear loves!” cried Flore gayly, “the salmi is getting cold. Come, my old rat, here’s a wing for you,” she said, smiling on Jean-Jacques.

At the words the long-drawn face lost its cadaverous tints, a smile flickered on his lips; but he had another fit of coughing; joy excited as violent an emotion as punishment. Flore rose, pulled a little cashmere shawl from her shoulders, and tied it round his throat, exclaiming: “How silly to put yourself in such a way about nothing. There, you old goose, that will do you good; it has been next my heart—”

“What a good creature!” said Rouget to Max, while Flore fetched a black velvet cap for the nearly bald head.

“As good as she is beautiful,” answered Max, “but she is quick-tempered, like all people who carry their hearts in their hands.”

This bald sketch may displease some who think Flore’s flashes of character should be left in shadow. Well! this scene, played again and again with shocking variations, is, in its coarse way and horrible veracity, the type of scenes women enact on whatever social rung when any interest draws them from obedience and induces them to grasp power. In their eyes, as in politicians’, all means justify the end. Between Flore Brazier and a duchess, between a duchess and the richest bourgeoise, between a bourgeoise and the luxuriously kept mistress, differences are those of education and surroundings. The pouting of a fine lady is the same as the violence of a Rabouilleuse. At all levels, bitter sayings, ironical jests, cold contempt, hypocritical complaints, false quarrels win as much success as low outbursts of this Madame Everard of Issoudun.

Max began to relate, with humor, Fario and his barrow, making the old man laugh. Vedie and Kouski in the kitchen exploded, and Flore laughed convulsively. After breakfast, while Jean-Jacques read the newspapers (they subscribed to the Constitutionel and the Pandore), Max carried Flore to his quarters.

“Are you sure he has not made any other will since the one leaving property to you?”

“He hasn’t anything to write with,” she answered.

“He might have dictated it to some notary,” said Max; “we must look out for that. Therefore be cordial to the Bridaus, and at the same time turn those mortgages into money. The notaries will be glad to make the transfers; it is grist to their mill. The Funds are rising; we shall conquer Spain, deliver Ferdinand VII and the Cortez, and they will be above par. You and I could make a good thing by putting the old fellow’s seven hundred and fifty thousand francs into the Funds at eighty-nine. Only you must try to get it done in your name; it will be so much secured anyhow.”

“A capital idea!” said Flore.

“And as there will be an income of fifty thousand francs from eight hundred and ninety thousand, we must make him borrow one hundred and forty thousand francs for two years, to be paid back in two instalments. In two years, we shall get one hundred thousand francs in Paris, and ninety thousand here, and risk nothing.”

“If it were not for you, my handsome Max, what would become of me now?” she said.

“Oh! tomorrow night at Mere Cognette’s, after I have seen the Parisians, I shall find a way to make the Hochons themselves get rid of them.”

“Ah! what a head you’ve got, my angel! You are a love of a man.”

The place Saint-Jean is at the centre of a long street called at the upper end rue Grand Narette, and at the lower rue Petite Narette. “Narette” in Berry expresses a steep street. The Grand Narette rises rapidly from place Saint-Jean to the port Vilatte. The house of old Monsieur Hochon is exactly opposite Jean-Jacques Rouget’s. From Madame Hochon’s usual sitting-room it was easy to see what went on at the Rouget household, and vice versa, when curtains were drawn back or doors left open. The Hochon house resembled the Rouget house; doubtless the same architect built both. Monsieur Hochon, formerly tax-collector at Selles in Berry, born at Issoudun, had returned home and married the sister of the sub-delegate, the gay Lousteau, exchanging his office at Selles for one at Issoudun. Having retired before 1787, he escaped the Revolution’s dangers, to whose principles, however, he adhered like other “honest men” who howl with the winners. Monsieur Hochon had the reputation of miser; a single episode will show him.

At his daughter’s wedding, necessary to give a dinner to the Borniche family, the bridegroom, heir to a large fortune, had mismanaged his property and been mortified by his parents’ refusal to help. The old people were delighted to see Monsieur Hochon step in as guardian to secure his daughter’s dowry. While the marriage contract was solemnly read by young Heron, the notary, the cook came in and asked Monsieur Hochon for some twine to truss the turkey—an essential of the repast. The old man dove into his coat and gave an end of string already used to tie a parcel, and before she left the room called out, “Gritte, mind you give it back to me!” (Gritte is Berry for Marguerite.)

Year by year Hochon grew more petty and penurious; at eighty-five he belonged to men who stop mid-conversation to pick up a pin, remarking as they stick it in their coat sleeve, “There’s the wife’s stipend.” He complained of cloth quality and how his coat had lasted ten years. Tall, gaunt, thin, sallow; saying little, reading little, doing nothing to fatigue himself; observant of forms, he enforced at home strict abstemious discipline, weighing and measuring food and drink for his family: his wife née Lousteau, his grandson Borniche with a sister Adolphine, heirs of old Borniche, and his other grandson Francois Hochon.

Hochon’s eldest son, taken by the 1813 draft which drew sons of well-to-do families into the “guards of honor,” was killed at Hanau. He had married early a rich woman to avoid conscription; after enrolment he wasted his substance, foreseeing his end. His wife, who followed the army at a distance, died at Strasburg in 1814, leaving debts Hochon refused to pay—answering creditors with an axiom: “Women are minors.”

The house, though large, was scantily furnished; on the second floor two rooms suited Madame Bridau and Joseph. Old Hochon repented having kept them with two beds, each with an old armchair of natural wood covered with needlework, and a walnut table with a wide-mouthed water-pitcher in a basin. He kept winter stores of apples, pears, medlars, and quinces on heaps of straw in these rooms where rats and mice ran riot, exhaling a mixed odor of fruit and vermin. Madame Hochon directed everything be cleaned; wallpaper fastened with wafers; she decorated windows with curtains pieced from old hoards. Denied a strip of drugget, she laid her bedside carpet for little Agathe—“Poor little thing!” she said—who was now over forty-seven. Madame Hochon borrowed two night-tables from a neighbor and boldly hired two chests of drawers with brass handles from a second-hand dealer next to Mere Cognette. She had preserved two candlesticks carved in choice woods by her father, who had the “turning” mania; from 1770 to 1780 it was fashionable for rich people to learn a trade, and Monsieur Lousteau had been a turner. These candlesticks were ornamented with circlets from roots of rose, peach, and apricot trees. Madame Hochon actually risked using her relics. These preparations and sacrifices increased old Hochon’s anxiety; until now he had not believed the Bridaus’ arrival.

The morning of the Fario trick Madame Hochon said after breakfast,—

“I hope, Hochon, you will receive my goddaughter, Madame Bridau, properly.” Then, making sure her grandchildren were out of hearing, she added, “I am mistress of my own property; don’t oblige me to make up to Agathe in my will for any incivility on your part.”

“Do you think, madame,” answered Hochon mildly, “that at my age I don’t know forms of decent civility?”

“You know very well what I mean, you crafty old thing! Be friendly to our guests, and remember I love Agathe.”

“And you love Maxence Gilet also, who is getting the property away from your dear Agathe! Ah! you’ve warmed a viper in your bosom; but after all, the Rouget money is bound to go to a Lousteau.”

Having said this about Max and Agathe, Hochon turned to leave; but Madame Hochon, still erect and spare, wearing a round cap with ribbon knots, powdered hair, a taffeta petticoat of changeable colors like a pigeon’s breast, tight sleeves, and high-heeled slippers, placed her snuff-box on a little table and said,—

“Really, Monsieur Hochon, how can a man of your sense repeat absurdities which cost my poor friend her peace and Agathe the property she ought to have had from her father. Max Gilet is not the son of my brother, whom I often advised to save the money he paid for him. You know as well as I do that Madame Rouget was virtue itself—”

“And the daughter takes after her; for she strikes me as uncommonly stupid. After losing all her fortune, she brings her sons up so well that here is one in prison and likely to be indicted before the Court of Peers for a conspiracy worthy of Berton. As for the other, he is worse—a painter. If your protégés stay here till they have extricated that fool Rouget from Gilet and the Rabouilleuse, we shall eat a good deal more than half a measure of salt with them.”

“That’s enough, Monsieur Hochon; you had better wish they may not have two strings to their bow.”

Monsieur Hochon took his hat and his cane with an ivory knob and went away petrified by that speech; he had no idea his wife could show such resolution. Madame Hochon took her prayer-book to read the service; her age prevented daily church attendance; she got there with difficulty on Sundays. Since receiving her goddaughter’s letter she had added a petition to her prayers, supplicating God to open Jean-Jacques Rouget’s eyes, bless Agathe, and prosper the expedition she had drawn her into. Concealing the fact from her grandchildren, whom she accused of being “parpaillots,” she had asked the curate to say a mass for Agathe’s success during a neuvaine held by her granddaughter Adolphine Borniche, who thus made her prayers in church by proxy.

Adolphine, then eighteen, who for seven years had sewn at her grandmother’s side in that cold household of monotony and method, undertook the neuvaine hoping to inspire some feeling in Joseph Bridau, in whom she took an interest because of the monstrosities her grandfather attributed to the young Parisian.

All the old and sensible people of the town and fathers of families approved Madame Hochon’s conduct in receiving her goddaughter; their good wishes for her success matched the secret contempt for Maxence Gilet’s conduct. Thus news of Rouget’s sister and nephew raised two parties in Issoudun—the higher and older bourgeoisie, who offered good wishes and watched without assisting, and the Knights of Idleness and Max’s partisans, who were capable of high-handed outrages against the Parisians.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
CHAPTER XI

Agathe and Joseph arrived at the coach-office of the Messageries-Royales in the place Misere at three o’clock. Though tired, Madame Bridau felt her youth revive at sight of her native land, where each step recalled girlish memories. In Issoudun public opinion spread news quickly; the Parisians’ arrival was known in ten minutes. Madame Hochon came to the door to welcome her godchild and kissed her as if she were a daughter. After seventy-two years of a barren life, marked by the graves of three unhappy, dead children, she had cherished this early friendship as if Agathe had remained near her, and she had also taken the deepest interest in Bridau. Agathe was led triumphant into the salon where Monsieur Hochon sat, chilling as a tepid oven.

“Here is Monsieur Hochon; how does he seem to you?” his wife asked.

“Precisely the same as when I last saw him,” said Agathe.

“Ah! it is easy to see you come from Paris; you are so complimentary,” remarked the old man.

Presentations followed: first Baruch Borniche, a tall youth of twenty-two; then Francois Hochon, twenty-four; and lastly little Adolphine, blushing and awkward, anxious not to seem to look at Joseph Bridau, who in turn was narrowly observed from different points of view by the two young men and old Hochon. The miser thought, “He’s just out of the hospital; he’ll be as hungry as a convalescent.” The young men muttered, “What a head! what a brigand! we shall have our hands full!”

“This is my son, the painter; my good Joseph,” said Agathe, presenting him. The emphasis on “good” betrayed the mother’s heart, whose thoughts were really in the Luxembourg prison.

“He looks ill,” said Madame Hochon; “he is not at all like you.”

“No, madame,” said Joseph, with the brusque candor of an artist; “I am like my father, and very ugly at that.”

Madame Hochon pressed Agathe’s hand and gazed at her as if to say, “Ah! my child; I understand now why you prefer your good-for-nothing Philippe.”

“I never saw your father, my dear boy,” she added aloud; “it is enough to make me love you that you are your mother’s son. Besides, you have talent, so the late Madame Descoings used to write to me; she was the only one in late years who told me much about you.”

“Talent!” exclaimed Joseph, “not yet; but with time and patience I may win fame and fortune.”

“By painting?” Monsieur Hochon sneered.

“Come, Adolphine,” said Madame Hochon, “go and see about dinner.”

“Mother,” said Joseph, “I will attend to the trunks.”

“Hochon,” said the grandmother to Francois, “show the rooms to Monsieur Bridau.”

It was half past three and dinner at four; Baruch rushed into town to tell of the Bridau arrival, describe Agathe’s dress, and especially paint Joseph’s haggard, determined face—like a brigand’s. That evening Joseph was talked about in every household.

“That sister of Rouget must have seen a monkey before her son was born,” said one; “he’s the image of a baboon.”

“He has the face of a brigand and the eyes of a basilisk.”

“All artists are like that.”

“They are spiteful as monkeys.”

“I have just seen Monsieur Beaussier, and he says he would not like to meet him in a dark wood; he saw him in the diligence.”

“He has hollows over the eyes like a horse, and he laughs like a maniac.”

“The fellow looks capable of anything; perhaps he ruined his fine brother. Poor Madame Bridau doesn’t seem happy with him.”

“Suppose we take advantage of his being here, and have our portraits painted?”

Curiosity flared. Those entitled to visit the Hochons resolved to call that night and examine the Parisians. Their arrival in the stagnant town was like dropping a beam among frogs.

After stowing trunks into the attic chambers, which he inspected, Joseph noted the silent house—the walls, staircase, woodwork utterly plain and damp, with nothing beyond bare necessities. He felt the sharp transition from poetic Paris to arid province; coming downstairs and seeing Monsieur Hochon slicing bread for each person, he understood Moliere’s Harpagon.

“We should have done better to go to an inn,” he thought.

Dinner confirmed his fears. A watery soup showed quantity favored over quality; bouilli was ceremoniously garnished with parsley and held the table’s center. Vegetables were served separately and counted as items of the repast. The bouilli’s place was accompanied by three other dishes: hard-boiled eggs on sorrel, a salad dressed with nut-oil, and little cups of custard flavored with burnt oats serving for vanilla, like chicory coffee to mocha. Butter and radishes were at each end, with pickled gherkins and horseradish; Madame Hochon approved, nodding contentedly at her husband. He answered with a shrug that meant, “See the extravagances you force me to commit!”

After Monsieur Hochon slivered the bouilli into slices about as thick as a dancing-shoe sole, it was replaced with three pigeons. The wine was country vintage 1811. Adolphine had decorated each end of the table with a bunch of flowers.

“At Rome as the Romans do,” thought the artist, and began to eat—like a man who had breakfasted at Vierzon on an execrable cup of coffee. When Joseph, having eaten all his bread, asked for more, Monsieur Hochon slowly searched his pockets for a key, unlocked a cupboard, broke off a section of a twelve-pound loaf, cut a round, divided it, laid the pieces on a plate, and passed it across the table with the silence of an old soldier meeting death calmly. Joseph took the half-slice and understood that asking for more was forbidden. No one was surprised. Conversation went on. Agathe learned that her birth house had been bought by the Borniches; she wished to see it.

“No doubt,” said her godmother, “the Borniches will be here this evening; we shall have half the town—who want to examine you,” she added, turning to Joseph, “and they will all invite you.”

Gritte, the sixty-year-old servant, brought dessert—the famous ripe cheese of Touraine and Berry, with mouldy discolorations resembling vine-leaves; she placed nuts and time-honored biscuits beside them.

“Well, Gritte, the fruit?” asked Madame Hochon.

“But, madame, there is none rotten,” answered Gritte.

Joseph burst into roars of laughter as among his atelier comrades; he perceived that parsimony in eating only fruits beginning to rot had become settled habit.

“Bah! we can eat them all the same,” he exclaimed.

“Monsieur Hochon, pray get some,” said the old lady.

Monsieur Hochon, much incensed, fetched peaches, pears, and Saint Catherine plums.

“Adolphine, go and gather some grapes,” Madame Hochon ordered her granddaughter.

Joseph looked at the two young men as if to say: “Is it to such fare you owe your healthy faces?” Baruch smiled; he and his cousin had been moderate and frequented Mere Cognette’s for feasts. Just before dinner, Baruch had been told the grand master convoked the Order at midnight for a magnificent supper and a great enterprise. The feast of welcome at the Hochons explains the need for nocturnal repasts for two young fellows blessed with appetites, who never missed them.

“We will take the liqueur in the salon,” said Madame Hochon, rising and motioning Joseph to give her his arm. As they went out before the others she whispered to him:

“Eh! my poor boy; this dinner won’t give you indigestion; I had hard work to get it for you. It is always Lent here; you will get enough just to keep life in you, and no more. So you must bear it patiently.”

The old woman’s kindness pleased Joseph.

“I have lived fifty years with that man, without ever hearing half-a-dozen gold pieces chink in my purse,” she went on. “Oh! if I did not hope that you might save your property, I would never have brought you and your mother into my prison.”

“But how can you survive it?” cried Joseph naively.

“Ah, you may well ask!” she said. “I pray.”

Joseph quivered at the words, which raised her in his estimation; he stepped back to look into her face. “Let me paint your portrait,” he said.

“No, no,” she answered, “I am too weary of life to wish to remain here on canvas.”

Gayly uttering the sad thing, she opened a closet and brought out a flask of ratafia she made from a nuns’ recipe, the same source for Issoudun’s celebrated cake; Monsieur de Riviere ordered quantities yearly for the Seraglio.

Adolphine held a lacquer tray of little engraved glasses; as her mother filled and handed them around, Agathe remarked, “It seems as though my father’s turn were coming round!” This immutable provincial custom recalled her youth.

“Hochon will go to his club presently to read the papers, and we shall have a little time to ourselves,” said the old lady quietly.

Ten minutes later three women and Joseph were alone in the salon, where the floor was only swept, and the worsted-work designs and plain, dismal furniture seemed exactly as when Agathe left. Monarchy, Revolution, Empire, and Restoration had left no trace in this room.

“Ah! my godmother, compared with your life, mine has been cruelly tried,” exclaimed Madame Bridau, surprised to find even a canary she knew stuffed on the mantelshelf among the old clock, brass brackets, and silver candlesticks.

“My child,” said the old lady, “trials are in the heart. The greater the need for resignation, the harder the struggle with ourselves. But don’t speak of me, let us talk of your affairs. You are directly in front of the enemy,” she added, pointing to the Rouget windows.

“They are sitting down to dinner,” said Adolphine.

The young girl, destined for a cloister, looked constantly out the window, hoping to hear more about the enormities imputed to Maxence Gilet, the Rabouilleuse, and Jean-Jacques. The old lady told her granddaughter to leave her and Agathe alone until visitors arrived. “We shall have ten or twelve batches of inquisitive folk here tonight,” she said.

Madame Hochon then recounted Maxence’s influence over Jean-Jacques Rouget and the Rabouilleuse’s doings, with all the town’s embroidered comments. Adolphine announced the approach of the Borniche, Beaussier, Lousteau-Prangin, Fichet, Goddet-Herau families—fourteen persons in all.

“You now see, my dear child,” said the old lady, “that it will not be easy to get this property out of the wolf’s jaws—”

“It seems so difficult—with a scoundrel such as you represent him, and a daring woman like that crab-girl—as to be actually impossible,” Joseph remarked. “We should have to stay a year in Issoudun to counteract their influence and overthrow their dominion over my uncle. Money isn’t worth such a struggle—not to speak of the meannesses to which we should have to stoop. My mother has only two weeks’ leave; her place is permanent and must not be risked. As for me, in October I have an important work Schinner has obtained for me from a peer of France; my future fortune is in my brushes.”

Madame Hochon was amazed; though relatively superior to the town, she did not believe in painting. She glanced at her goddaughter and pressed her hand.

“This Maxence is the second volume of Philippe,” whispered Joseph to his mother, “only cleverer and better behaved. Well, madame,” he added aloud, “we won’t trouble Monsieur Hochon by staying long.”

“Ah! you are young; you know nothing of the world,” said the old lady. “A couple of weeks, if judicious, may produce great results; listen to my advice, and act accordingly.”

“Oh! willingly,” said Joseph. “I know I have an amazing incapacity for domestic statesmanship: for example, I’m sure I don’t know what Desroches himself would tell us to do if my uncle declines to see us.”

Mesdames Borniche, Goddet-Herau, Beaussier, Lousteau-Prangin and Fichet, with their husbands, entered the room.

When the fourteen were seated and compliments over, Madame Hochon presented Agathe and Joseph. Joseph sat in his armchair all evening, slyly studying the sixty faces which, from five until half-past nine, posed for him gratis, as he later told his mother. Such behavior before Issoudun’s aristocracy did not change its opinion of him: everyone went home ruffled by his sarcastic glances, uneasy under his smiles, and even frightened at his sinister face, which a class unable to recognize genius found alarming.

After ten, when the household slept, Madame Hochon kept her goddaughter in her chamber until midnight. Secure from interruption the two women exchanged sorrows. As Agathe listened to the last echoes of a soul that had missed its destiny and felt the sufferings of a generous heart whose charity could never be exercised, she perceived the immensity of the desert in which this noble, unrecognized soul had been wasted, and knew that she, with city life’s small joys, was not the most unhappy.

“You who are so pious,” she said, “explain my shortcomings; tell me what God punishes in me.”

“He is preparing us, my child,” answered the old woman, “for the striking of the last hour.”

At midnight the Knights of Idleness collected one by one like shadows under the boulevard Baron trees, whispering.

“What are we going to do?” each asked on arriving.

“I think,” said Francois, “Max means merely to give us a supper.”

“No; matters are serious for him and the Rabouilleuse: he has concocted some scheme against the Parisians.”

“It would be a good joke to drive them away.”

“My grandfather,” said Baruch, “is terribly alarmed at having two extra mouths to feed, and would seize any pretext—”

“Well, comrades!” cried Max softly, now appearing, “why star-gazing? the planets don’t distil kirschwasser. Come, let us go to Mere Cognette’s!”

“To Mere Cognette’s! To Mere Cognette’s!” they all cried.

The cry rang through the town like troops rushing; silence followed. The next day many asked, “Did you hear those frightful cries last night about one? I thought there was a fire.”

A supper worthy of La Cognette brightened the faces of twenty-two guests; the whole Order was present. At two, as they began to siroter, Max rose to speak:

“My dear fellows! the honor of your grand master was grossly attacked this morning after our memorable joke with Fario’s cart—attacked by a vile peddler, and what is more, a Spaniard (oh, Cabrera!); and I have resolved to make the scoundrel feel my vengeance—always, of course, within our rules. After reflecting, I have a trick worth execution—a famous trick that will drive him crazy. While avenging the insult, we shall feed the sacred animals of the Egyptians—little beasts unjustly persecuted. Thus good is the child of evil. I order you all, under pain of displeasing your humble grand master, to procure clandestinely, each, twenty rats, male or female. Collect your contingent within three days. Keep the rodents without food so they are ravenous. I will accept house-mice and field-mice as rats. Twenty-two times twenty gives four hundred; four hundred let loose in the old Capuchin church where Fario stores his grain will eat a not insignificant quantity! Be lively; Fario delivers most grain soon, and I am determined that Spaniard shall find a terrible deficit. I have not the merit of this invention,” Max added, seeing admiration; “render to Caesar that which is Caesar’s. My scheme reproduces Samson’s foxes; but Samson was an incendiary, no philanthropist; we, like Brahmins, protect a persecuted race. Mademoiselle Flore Brazier has set mouse-traps, and Kouski hunts field-mice. I have spoken.”

“I know,” said Goddet, “where to find an animal worth forty rats himself alone.”

“What’s that?”

“A squirrel.”

“I offer a little monkey,” said a younger member, “he’ll make himself drunk on wheat.”

“Bad!” exclaimed Max, “it would show who put the beasts there.”

“But we might each catch a pigeon some night,” said young Beaussier, “taking them from different farms; if we put them through a hole in the roof, they’ll attract thousands.”

“So, then, for the next week, Fario’s storehouse is the order of the night,” cried Max. “Recollect; people rise early in Saint-Paterne. Also, none of you go there without turning the soles of your list shoes backward. Knight Beaussier, the inventor of pigeons, is made director. I shall leave my imprint on the sacks of wheat. Gentlemen, you are appointed to the commissariat of the Army of Rats. If you find a watchman sleeping in the church, you must make him drunk—and cleverly—so he is far from the Rodents’ Orgy.”

“You don’t say anything about the Parisians?” Goddet asked.

“Oh!” exclaimed Max, “I want time to study them. Meantime, I offer my best shotgun—the one the Emperor gave me, a treasure from Versailles—to whoever finds a way to play the Bridaus a trick that will get them into difficulties with Madame and Monsieur Hochon, so those worthy people will send them off, or the Parisians will be forced to go of their own accord—without injuring the venerable ancestors of my two friends here, Baruch and Francois.”

“All right! I’ll think of it,” said Goddet, who coveted the gun.

“If the inventor of the trick doesn’t want the gun, he shall have my horse,” added Max.

After that night twenty brains were tortured to lay a plot against Agathe and her son, following Max’s programme. But only the devil or chance could help them succeed; the conditions made it well-nigh impossible.

The next morning Agathe and Joseph came down before the second breakfast at ten. In Hochon’s household first breakfast meant a cup of milk and a slice of bread and butter taken in bed or when rising. While waiting for Madame Hochon to finish her minute toilette, Joseph noticed Jean-Jacques Rouget planted squarely at his door across the street. He pointed him out; Agathe could hardly recognize her brother, so altered was he.

“That is your brother,” said Adolphine.

“What an idiot he looks like!” exclaimed Joseph.

Agathe clasped her hands and looked to heaven. “What a state they have driven him to! Good God! can that be a man only fifty-seven?”

She looked closely and saw Flore Brazier standing behind him, hair dressed, snowy shoulders and a dazzling bosom through a gauze neckerchief trimmed with lace; her grenadine dress had leg-of-mutton sleeves fastened at the wrists with bracelets. A gold chain rippled over her bosom as she leaned forward to give Jean-Jacques his black silk cap; the scene was evidently studied.

“Hey!” cried Joseph, “there’s a fine woman, and a rare one! She’s made, as they say, to paint. What flesh-tints! Oh, the lovely tones! what surface! what curves! Ah, those shoulders! She’s a magnificent caryatid. What a model she would have been for one of Titian’s Venuses!”

Adolphine and Madame Hochon thought him talking Greek; Agathe signed behind his back that she was used to such jargon.

“So you think a creature who is depriving you of your property handsome?” said Madame Hochon.

“That doesn’t prevent her from being a splendid model—just plump enough not to spoil the hips and contour—”

“My son, you are not in your studio,” said Agathe. “Adolphine is here.”

“Ah, true! I did wrong. But you must remember that since leaving Paris I’ve seen nothing but ugly women—”

“My dear godmother,” Agathe said hastily, “how shall I meet my brother if that creature is always with him?”

“Bah!” said Joseph. “I’ll go and see him myself. I don’t think him such an idiot now I find he has the sense to rejoice his eyes with a Titian’s Venus.”

“If he were not an idiot,” said Monsieur Hochon, who had come in, “he would have married long ago and had children; and then you would have no chance at the property. It is an ill wind that blows no good.”

“Your son’s idea is very good,” said Madame Hochon; “he ought to pay the first visit. He can make his uncle understand that if you call there he must be alone.”

“That will affront Mademoiselle Brazier,” said old Hochon. “No, no, madame; swallow the pill. If you can’t get the whole property, secure a small legacy.”

The Hochons were not clever enough to match Max. In the middle of breakfast Kouski brought a letter from Monsieur Rouget to his sister, Madame Bridau. Madame Hochon made her husband read it aloud:

My dear Sister,—I learn from strangers of your arrival in Issoudun. I can guess the reason which made you prefer Monsieur and Madame Hochon’s house to mine; but if you will come to see me you shall be received as you ought. I should certainly pay you the first visit if my health did not compel me to keep the house; for which I offer my affectionate regrets. I shall be delighted to see my nephew, whom I invite to dine with me tomorrow—young men are less sensitive than women about the company. It will please me if Messrs. Baruch Borniche and Francois Hochon will accompany him.

Your affectionate brother,

J.-J. Rouget.

“Say we are at breakfast, but Madame Bridau will send an answer presently, and the invitations are all accepted,” said Monsieur Hochon to the servant.

The old man put a finger to his lips for silence. When the street-door closed, Monsieur Hochon, little suspecting the intimacy between his grandsons and Max, threw one of his slyest looks at his wife and Agathe, remarking, “He is just as capable of writing that note as I am of giving away twenty-five louis; it is the soldier who corresponds with us!”

“What does that portend?” asked Madame Hochon. “Well, never mind; we will answer him. As for you, monsieur,” she added to Joseph, “you must dine there; but if—”

The old lady was stopped by a look from her husband. Knowing her affection for Agathe, old Hochon feared she might leave some legacy to her goddaughter if Agathe lost the Rouget property. Though fifteen years older, the miser hoped to inherit her fortune and eventually control their property. That hope was fixed. Madame Hochon knew threatening him with her will was the best way to get concessions. Monsieur Hochon now sided with his guests. An enormous fortune was at stake; with a sense of social justice he wished it to go to natural heirs rather than pillaged by outsiders. The sooner the matter was decided, the sooner he would rid himself of the guests. The struggle between interlopers and heirs had become a fact, and Monsieur Hochon’s keen intelligence, lulled by provincial monotony, was roused. Madame Hochon was pleasantly surprised to find such an able auxiliary on the Bridau side.

Towards midday the brains of Monsieur and Madame Hochon, Agathe, and Joseph—Joseph amazed at the old people’s scrupulous choice of words—produced the following answer, concocted for Max and Flore:
